HEADLINE: Gritty Book Sets Prudence Aside

BYLINE: By DINITIA SMITH 

 BODY:
"Don't approach history as something to be shaken for its cautionary fruits," the author Tobias Wolff wrote to Mary Karr in January 1991, as she embarked on "The Liars' Club," her memoir of her East Texas childhood, published this month by Viking. "Don't be afraid of appearing angry, small-minded, obtuse, mean, immoral, amoral, calculating, or anything else," Mr. Wolff wrote. "Take no care for your dignity." And indeed, Ms. Karr did not. She has written one of the rawest memoirs by a serious American writer in years: the story of her childhood in "the Texas Ringworm Belt," oil-refinery country where the cancer rate rivals Bhopal's and Chernobyl's, in a town Business Week called one of the 10 ugliest on the planet.
Ms. Karr's mother suffered from something known locally as "Nervous." She married seven times -- twice to Ms. Karr's father -- and spent stretches of Ms. Karr's childhood insensible from drugs and alcohol. She set fire to Ms. Karr's toys, and tried to kill her. And as if all this wasn't enough to make a person miserable, at the age of 7 Ms. Karr was raped by a neighborhood boy and a short while later molested by her babysitter, all of which she discusses explicitly. Indeed, she takes no care for her dignity in the memoir, which Michiko Kakutani, reviewing it in The New York Times, called "an astonishing book." 
 Still, for someone who minces no words on paper, Ms. Karr is oddly private in person. "At this time I feel very protective of my 74-year-old mother," she said recently on a visit to New York from her job as a professor of creative writing and literature at Syracuse University. (Mr. Wolff, who wrote about his own harsh upbringing in "This Boy's Life," is a colleague.) She will not reveal the true name of the town that became Leechfield, Tex., in her memoir. Nor will she talk much about her own drinking, except to say, "I can't drink alcohol." And she will not discuss her drug taking, though she mentions it in her book, or the possibility that she may have spent time in a recovery program. "You're not supposed to say," she said.
For anyone who doesn't come from East Texas, Ms. Karr's memoir is like an anthropologist's account of a foreign country. This is a place where people "hunker down," and "hork" hot dogs, and insult is an art form. "You could see evil in the crotch of a tree," Ms. Karr's mother says to someone who has criticized her. And when a local man wakes from a coma mentally impaired, he is "half a bubble off-plumb."
Ms. Karr's book has a cast of Southern eccentrics right out of a Flannery O'Connor story. Her mother read Sartre and Camus, and told her daughters bedtime stories about Athens in the age of Socrates and fin-de-siecle Vienna. Ms. Karr's father, an oil driller, was a storyteller too, a man who shaped raw experience into the rise and fall of narrative, who took a poet's joy in the language, and who let his daughter listen in when he was with his drinking buddies, the Liars' Club.
Mary Karr went on to lead a wild life of her own. She left home at 17, fled to California and camped out with a group of surfers in a Lincoln Continental.
Only when she was grown, and rummaging in the attic for some papers, did she confront the secret that shaped her family: the existence of two children kidnapped from her mother by one of her husbands, who then disappeared. Suddenly, the reasons for her mother's need for oblivion, and her many marriages, became clear. Her mother had "started marrying people to get her kids back," Ms. Karr said. The night of Ms. Karr's discovery, Ms. Karr and her mother got drunk. "Why didn't you tell me?," Ms. Karr demanded. According to the memoir, her mother replied, "I thought you wouldn't like me anymore."
"Her exact sentence stays lodged in my head," Ms. Karr writes, "for it's one of the most pathetic sentences a 60-year-old woman can be caught uttering."
Partly out of a desire to shape this chaos into meaning, Ms. Karr always wanted to be a writer. She published a poem in Mother Jones magazine, a result of a chance encounter with the poet Denise Levertov. She was admitted to a writing course at Goddard College, where she met Mr. Wolff and wrote an essay on the poet John Ashbery's use of "non-linear" time. She moved to Boston, where she was a marketing communications manager for a computer company in Cambridge and a research fellow in managerial economics at the Harvard Business School. "If you're a poor kid you do what you have to do to make money," Ms. Karr said. Still, she managed to publish two books of poetry.
Along the way, she married a man from a very different background, whose idea of family life, she writes, was to speak to "his mother on holidays, from one end of a long, glossy dinner table." She will not reveal his identity, except to say that he went to Harvard, is a poet and teaches at a prep school in Connecticut. The marriage produced a son, Devereux Milburn, now 12. Three years ago, the marriage broke up.
Few poets can live off poetry. Ms. Karr had been trying to write a novel about her crazy childhood, without success. In 1989, both she and Mr. Wolff won $25,000 Whiting Writers' Awards, cash prizes given annually to 10 promising writers. At a dinner with Mr. Wolff and his agent, Amanda Urban, she told some of her stories about her family. Ms. Urban was intrigued, and urged her to write a memoir, asking for a proposal, three chapters and a delivery date.
"I realized 'She's serious as a heart attack,' " Ms. Karr said in her throaty voice, adding that Ms. Urban got her a sizable advance ("a whole lot; I'm not supposed to say how much"). With every three chapters, she said, "Viking would give me more."
In a way, "The Liars' Club" is a helpless child's answer to a mother before whom she is powerless. Ms. Karr's quest for the truth about her family was a search for salvation. And in a way it redeemed her mother's life as well.
After the book was finished, her mother demanded to read it. "She lobbied like a banshee to see it: 'What if I die before it's done?' " Ms. Karr said, imitating her mother's Texas drawl. Last August, on the back porch of Ms. Karr's house in Syracuse, her mother sat "turning the pages and weeping" and apologizing for her actions while her grandson tried to console her.
"I said, 'You were an alcoholic,' " Ms. Karr recalled. " 'Plus, you were really nuts!' She just thought it was great. She reads well. She doesn't suffer fools. She laughed and wept. My mother is one of the more unconventional human units."
Her mother is sober now. "She's really kind of an amazing human being," Ms. Karr said. "She's been sober for 12 or 13 years. She went back to school before my dad died. She taught art in junior high school. She did most of the work for her Ph.D. A lot of this came from finding those children. My half-brother helped her get sober."
Ms. Karr refused to discuss her newly found half-brother and half-sister, except to say their reunion has been a happy one. Her mother remains "both very capricious and a delightable human being," Ms. Karr said. "That makes her irresponsible."
"If I hadn't discovered the past and gone through therapy, we wouldn't be close," Ms. Karr said. At the end of the book, there is a soaring passage about the redemptive power of truth. She writes that "what mother told us absolved us. . . . All the black crimes we believed ourselves guilty of were myths. . . . "
"It's only looking back that I believe the clear light of truth should have filled us, like the legendary grace that carries a broken body past all manner of monsters."
These days, Mary Karr, sober and the mother of an active child, says she's happy. Will she marry again? "I hope not!" she said. "I'd rather take a whipping. I'm doing something I call Date-a-rama," she said.
"I saw in some review that I was born unlucky," she said. "When I hear myself described in these bathetic terms, I think of kids who are in Rwanda or H.I.V. positive. I get up, suit up, and shut up. I teach two afternoons a week; it's a piece of cake. I live in the house Toby lived in when he wrote 'This Boy's Life.' We put a basketball hoop up in the driveway; kids come from all over. Who wouldn't be happy!"
After a pause she added, "One reason I'm happy is I think I should've been dead a hundred times over."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mary Karr during a visit to New York City this month for the publication of her memoir. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY'S BUDGET: THE OVERVIEW;
LEGISLATORS PASS A TAX CUT OF 30% FOR NEW JERSEY

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, June 26

 BODY:
The New Jersey Legislature passed the second half of Gov. Christine Todd Whitman's 30 percent income tax reduction tonight, a year ahead of schedule, with many Democrats joining Republican majorities in the Assembly and Senate.
The Senate enacted the Governor's $16 billion budget with a party line vote of 22 to 17, and at 8:10 P.M. the Assembly passed the budget, 54 to 23, as well as the tax cut. 
 With her program winning approval, Governor Whitman scored the triumph of her young political career. Her promise to cut the income tax rate by nearly a third, which in 1993 had appeared to be a last-ditch move to jump-start a lagging campaign for governor, has propelled the telegenic, blunt-talking 48-year-old first termer to the upper ranks of the national Republican Party. If anything, she has augmented that reputation by delivering the $1.2 billion annual tax savings this year, a year ahead of her original three-year deadline pledge.
Governor Whitman is expected to sign the budget legislation this week.
The first 15 percent tax cut was enacted last year.
"Passage of this bill fulfills an historic promise Governor Whitman and the Republican Party made to the people of New Jersey in 1993, a promise skeptics said we could not or would not keep," said Chuck Haytaian, the Assembly speaker, who as an early supporter of the Governor's campaign pledge could also savor the triumph tonight.
Mr. Haytaian hammered the tax cut through the Assembly in the early afternoon on a vote of 63 to 14. In the end, 12 of the Assembly's 28 Democrats who are up for re-election in November denounced aspects of the Governor's tax cut but voted with her. Two other Democrats abstained.
Most of the 14 Democrats voting against the tax cut came from safe Democratic districts in the cities and inner suburbs, where the tax cut has been widely criticized as a gift to the rich.
"This is not a tax cut that will really enable us to attract new jobs and stimulate economic growth," Assemblywoman Loretta Weinberg, a Teaneck Democrat, said. "It is just another case of exploiting the exploited." Later she added, "It is the embodiment of a philosophy that says that there is a virtue in greed."
Even Joseph V. Doria Jr. of Bayonne, the Democratic minority leader, could not bring himself to vote against the tax cut and was one of two Democratic abstentions.
The steady progress of Mrs. Whitman's tax program, combined with an approval rating approaching 60 percent in the polls, has evaporated early skepticism while augmenting her reputation as a leader possessing equal measures of public warmth and political muscle.
It has spurred national Republican leaders to raise Mrs. Whitman's name as a potential vice presidential nominee next year, a line of thinking the Governor has never shut the door to.
Democrats in the Senate had hoped from the outset to exploit lingering fears among several leading Republicans that state revenues would fall short of covering the cost of the cuts, but the Republican leadership kept pushing them aside.
The Democratic Senate minority leader, John Lynch of New Brunswick, sought a quick ruling from the State Supreme Court today on Mrs. Whitman's refusal to release the text of a much-disputed letter from the Internal Revenue Service early in the day. The I.R.S. inquired in March about the status of $769 million taken from the state pension fund in 1992 and used to balance the budget. Mr. Lynch's Democrats conducted a mini-filibuster in hopes of delaying a vote on the tax cut until the court ruled on whether the nature of the I.R.S.'s inquiry should be made public.
When the Supreme Court sent the matter back to the trial court at 6:30 this evening, the Democrats tried unsuccessfully to delay the budget and tax cut votes until a meeting with the judges, but the Senate president, Donald T. DiFrancesco, insisted on a vote in time for the June 30 fiscal year deadline.
"The Governor has effectively thumbed her nose at the co-equal branches of government," Mr. Lynch said with some bitterness.
But even important members of the Republican majority had more concerns about the integrity of Governor Whitman's budget than about an I.R.S. issue that could, in any case, be drawn out over months if not years in litigation. Republican leaders such as Robert E. Littell, chairman of the Senate Budget Committee, have for several months worried about the impact a softening economy and possible Federal aid cuts would have on the Governor's precariously balanced budget.
In the end, however, Mr. Littell supported his Republican Governor, although the Senate quickly ran into a second impasse when the first vote on the budget measure stalled at 20 to 17, with 21 needed to carry. With one Senator out of state at his daughter's college graduation, two Republicans with heavy public employee constituencies, Richard J. LaRossa of Ewing and Peter A. Inverso of Hamilton refused to vote.
In a statement that brought him close to tears, Senator Inverso, who is vice chairman of the Senate Budget Committee, explained that he had spent the day seeking assurances from Governor Whitman that she would not veto language he had placed in the budget to sidetrack the Governor's plan to put the management of 23 motor vehicle offices under private management. This privatization measure would cost some 300 public employee jobs, many from his district.
"Unfortunately, I have been unable to get the assurances of the administration to abandon the privatization policy," Mr. Inverso said. Still, at the crucial moment, he and his neighbor in the adjoining desk, Senator LaRossa, pressed the green buttons on the desks to give the budget a 22 to 17 majority for passage.
During the debate, the two parties divided along two main themes, the Republicans calling the tax cut and the new budget a triumph for the state economy and the Democrats complaining that it was bought at the price of nearly $30 million in special appropriations -- pork, they called it -- for the home districts of influential members.
Critics have focused on the fact that the largest dollar savings under Mrs. Whitman's 30 percent income tax rate cut will go to families earning more than $85,000 a year, while the Whitman Administration has pointed out that the largest percentage reduction will go to those earning less than that amount. In addition, the cut removes all taxes on the earnings of 380,000 New Jerseyans who earn less than $7,500.
Just over 45 percent of all households in New Jersey make less than $80,000. A family earning that figure would see its state tax obligations decline to $1,470 from $2,100, a savings of $630, or the full 30 percent. At $200,000, a family's taxes would decline by 16.6 percent, to $8,379 from $10,050.
At $16 billion, Mrs. Whitman's budget is just $500 million higher than spending totals last year and the year before, in itself a striking example of the Governor's fiscal restraint in a state where budgets increased on average nearly $1 billion a year throughout the last decade.
During the give and take on the budget negotiations, Mrs. Whitman yielded on only a few points, agreeing to postpone $68 million in business taxes she had wanted and allowing the lawmakers to add the special benefits their home districts.
Although the day ended in triumph for the Governor, it was clear that several Republicans continued to have second thoughts about aspects of her plan.
"It is my hope that the negotiations and fine-tuning that have been under way for the past few weeks will continue beyond today's vote," Senator LaRossa said.

HEADLINE: Crackdown Has Cali Drug Cartel on the Run

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: CALI, Colombia, June 21

 BODY:
Until last month, Helmer Herrera Buitrago could relax in his penthouse bathtub while monitoring on closed circuit television who was coming and going from his private 14-story office and residential tower here.
To cruise around town, he could choose from a fleet of 70 cars, including a bullet-proofed Mazda equipped with louvers to allow his bodyguards to fire machine pistols at pursuers. 
 But hard times have fallen on this slender, mustachioed Colombian, whose personal empire allegedly includes much of the cocaine trade in New York and New Jersey. Over the last month, a special police squad has raided dozens of his businesses and homes, seizing cars, computer diskettes, bank statements, cellular phones and radio-activated beepers. Every day, television screens flash in bold red letters the reward for providing information that leads to the arrest of the kingpin -- $600,000.
"Pacho's getting desperate -- he can't take the pressure," a police lieutenant said, using Mr. Herrera's nickname. Colombian judicial authorities have said that his lawyers are trying to negotiate a surrender.
Two weeks after Gilberto Rodriguez Orejuela, known as the "chess master" of the Cali cocaine cartel, was captured cowering in a secret compartment in one of his houses, it appears that the glory days are over for the Cali drug traffickers, who earned an estimated $8 billion last year by controlling 80 percent of Colombia's cocaine exports.
Already there is evidence that the ability of the Cali cartel to refine cocaine and move it north -- it supplies most of the cocaine reaching the United States -- has been seriously disrupted. But whether the intensified law enforcement efforts here will reduce the quantity of the drug reaching the United States over time will depend on the ability of the Colombian Government to stamp out new cartels as they arise, and will also require si,ilar crackdowns in Bolivia, Mexico and Peru.
A turnaround in Colombia's policy started in January, when the new President, Ernesto Samper Pizano, realized that his laissez-faire attitude toward the traffickers could cause the United States, Colombia's largest trading partner and foreign investor, to impose economic sanctions. New policies were drawn up, but not in time to save Colombia from flunking an annual United States Government review of drug cooperation by its allies. Although Colombia was rated "uncooperative" on March 1, President Clinton waived sanctions on the ground of national interest.
 
Pressure by U.S. Brings Results
 The new get-tough policies have started to shake up Cali, which with two million people is Colombia's third largest city and which long profited from a cocaine-fueled investment boom. The capture of Gilberto Rodriguez Orejuela on June 9 was the culmination of three months of police pressure.
Since the 1970's, Colombian attacks on the drug business have been spasmodic, usually in response to heavy United States pressure. In one such concerted effort, the Government, with United States aid, has eradicated about one-quarter of the nation's coca leaf and opium poppy fields since January.
Despite this, the price of semi-refined cocaine is now half what it was in May. Colombian and American officials believe that this drop has come about because the Cali cartel's ability to arrange the financing, refining and transporting of drug shipments has been so severely disrupted.
So far, these officials believe, the American demand for cocaine is being met by stocks already in the United States. But they expect that the measures in Colombia will soon reduce the supply of cocaine reaching the United States and that cocaine prices there will rise later this summer as shortages move down the pipeline.
For the long term, the question is whether the Colombian Government will strike with equal force against smaller traffickers who try to replace the captured Cali bosses. Other questions are whether cocaine dealers in the two other producing countries, Peru and Bolivia, will develop the expertise and networks needed to meet the American demand, and whether Mexican dealers will seek a larger role in the trade.
The traffickers' fall has been swift.
Only six months ago, a high-ranking Colombian official telephoned Miguel Rodriguez Orejuela, one of Gilberto's two brothers, to apologize for the way soldiers and police officers interrupted his daughter's first communion party in a Cali hotel. Today, Miguel is a fugitive with a $2.5 million price on his head.
A year ago, Colombia's Prosecutor General met with Pacho Herrera, the supplier for the New York area, and issued him a certificate stating that no judicial investigations were pending against him. Apparently the official, Gustavo de Greiff, saw nothing amiss in the rapid growth of Mr. Herrera's business empire, including 12 ranches and 32 companies. Three months ago, however, the new Prosecutor General, Alfonso Valdivieso Sarmiento, issued an arrest warrant for Mr. Herrera on charges of drug trafficking and illicit enrichment.
"The entire effort of the Colombian police has picked up enormously since March," said Robert H. Gelbard, the State Department's top narcotics specialist.
 
Crackdown on Cartel Pleases Colombians
 The anti-cartel campaign has strong support at home. Last month, 76 percent of respondents to a nationwide poll said they supported it.
Kicking off the crackdown, on March 2, the police arrested Jorge Eliecer Rodriguez Orejuela, the youngest of the trafficking brothers. A notary public was dismissed from government service late the same month after Miguel Rodriguez Orejuela appeared before him to sign a power of attorney. Also in late March, a court administrator was arrested on charges of appointing a judge hand-picked by Miguel Rodriguez Orejuela to preside over his own drug-trafficking trial. He was to have been tried in absentia. The trial was moved to Bogota.
At the same time, an elite police "search brigade" conducted the first of more than 400 raids intended to dismantle the cartel's communications, transportation and security apparatus.
One target is the cartel-controlled taxi companies, where an estimated 1,500 drivers worked as informants for the traffickers. In raids on cartel offices, the police seized advanced communications devices -- radios that distort voices, scanners to intercept police radios, videophones for visual confirmation of callers, and devices for scrambling computer modem transmissions.
"The C.I.A. tells me that the sophistication of the Cali cartel was about at the level of the K.G.B. when the Soviet Union fell apart," Fernando Botero, Colombia's Defense Minister, said here recently.
 
U.S. Agents Provide Valuable Information
 The C.I.A. has loaned technicians to the search brigade, and American Drug Enforcement Administration agents stationed here help the effort by buying information. "The information they get is very valuable," Col. Misael Murcia, commander of the search brigade, said of the help provided by American agents.
The search brigade was formed in January 1994, but the cartel quickly tapped its telephones, making many raids ineffectual.
The unit now acts under the assumption that its telephones are tapped and that the national toll-free tipster numbers also are tapped.
Hard tips do make their way to the police here. Two tipsters split a $1.5 million reward for the information that led to the capture of Gilberto Rodriguez Orejuela. Buckling under the pressure, another cartel leader, Henry Loaiza Ceballos, surrendered on Monday, and a third, Phanor Arizabaleta Arzayus, said he would surrender next week.
[On Saturday, Victor Patino, a former police officer who rose to the No. 5 position in the gang, turned himself in.]
Determined to make more arrests by August, the first anniversary of Mr. Samper's administration, the Government is increasing the search brigade by 50 percent this month, to 9,000 police officers and soldiers.
Although a police intelligence officer was killed this month 50 miles north of here, the crackdown has been peaceful by Colombian standards. In contrast, Colombia's war on Pablo Escobar, the Medellin cartel leader, cost hundreds of lives until he was killed in December 1993.
 
Bribes, Not Bullets, Is Cartel's Policy
 "Escobar was a car thief, Rodriguez Gacha was a street punk," Colonel Murcia said of the Medellin cartel leaders. "The Rodriguez Orejuelas are very educated. It is not in their interest to use lead on us because, if they do, the Government will take everything away."
Indeed, the white-collar business leaders of the Cali cartel generally prefer bribes over bullets.
In a scandal that is rocking Colombia's governing Liberal Party, Mr. Valdivieso, the Prosecutor General, is investigating charges that the Cali cartel made payoffs to the Attorney General, the Comptroller General, the President of the House of Representatives and 14 other members of Congress.
Mr. Valdivieso, who occupies a politically independent post, is also investigating campaign contributions of $17 million made by cartel front companies during last year's congressional and presidential campaign season. Campaign aides to President Samper contend that Cali cartel figures offered to contribute, but that the money was refused.
"Their ace in the sleeve is what they have on the politicians," said a mid-level cartel employee. "They are going to negotiate. They are looking for sentences of eight or nine years, maximum."
An independent politician says that he met secretly last weekend with Miguel Rodriguez Orejuela. The politician, Bernardo Hoyos, said that Mr. Rodriguez Orejuela showed him photocopies of canceled checks totaling about $20 million written out to leading politicians. Mr. Hoyos would not cite names.
Under Colombia's existing plea bargaining and sentence reduction system, Gilberto Rodriguez Orejuela, who is 56, could spend as little as eight years in jail. The system is so generous that more than 1,000 Colombians have accepted plea bargain offers since the program started 18 months ago. The average sentence has been three years.
American officials warn that the crackdown on the Cali cartel could prove useless without stronger sentences and seizures of assets acquired through drug trafficking.
"These arrests are the first steps in a very long process," Myles R. Frechette, the United States Ambassador, said today. "There are other guys waiting for opportunities. The drug problem will take 15 to 20 years to solve in Colombia."

His brother Gilberto was arrested after he was found in a secret compartment in one of his houses. (Associated Press) (pg. A8)

HEADLINE: Clinton Calls U.N.'s Critics 'Isolationists'

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, June 26

 BODY:
Fifty years after the adoption of the United Nations Charter here, President Clinton fiercely defended the organization today against Congressional critics he branded "the new isolationists." But he also called for a streamlined United Nations that "wastes less and produces more."
Speaking on the same stage where delegates signed the United Nations Charter in 1945, Mr. Clinton recalled President Franklin D. Roosevelt's observation that America's well-being is dependent on the well-being of other nations, and said, "Today more than ever those words ring true." 
 "So I say, especially to the opponents of the United Nations here in the United States, turning our back on the U.N. and going it alone will lead to far more economic, political and military burdens on our people in the future and would ignore the lessons of our own history," the President said. "Let us say no to isolation, yes to reform."
Mr. Clinton 's address came at the end of a morning of pageantry, poetry and paeans to the United Nations in San Francisco's War Memorial Opera House, with much of the ceremony dwelling on the hopes of Mr. Roosevelt and President Harry S. Truman for a new global institution to avert another world war.
Maya Angelou read a poem for peace. And Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali called United Nations peacekeeping operations "a manifestation of the world's capacity to work together."
But crowds of demonstrators outside -- some waving "Save Bosnia" placards -- underscored that the anniversary celebration comes at a time when the 185-member organization is increasingly under fire from critics who see its peacekeeping mission in Bosnia as a failure.
Because of legislation passed last year, the United States plans to scale back its financial support for United Nations peacekeeping operations in October, so it pays a quarter of their costs instead of more than 30 percent. This year the Republican-dominated House of Representatives passed a bill that would cut financial support for current peacekeeping operations by more than $1 billion and would limit the President's power to approve new missions.
Mr. Clinton pledged to work to keep the United States paying "its fair share" of United Nations costs. But he also said the bureaucracy-bound institution must make the same efforts as governments and corporations to scale back its staff and set clearer priorities.
"Over the years it has grown too bloated, too often encouraging duplication, and spending resources on meetings rather than results," he said. "As its board of directors, all of us -- we the member states -- must create a U.N. that is more flexible, that operates more rapidly, that wastes less and produces more, and most importantly, that inspires confidence among our governments and our people."
Mr. Clinton said the United Nations could no longer afford a "separate agency with its own acronym, stationary and bureaucracy for every problem." And he called on the United Nations to be more selective in its peacekeeping missions.
"Peacekeeping can only succeed when the parties to a conflict understand they cannot profit from war," he said. "We have too often asked our peacekeepers to work miracles while denying them the military and political support required and the modern command-and-control systems they need to do their job as safely and effectively as possible."
Mr. Clinton made only a brief mention of the Bosnian conflict but instead dwelled on the organization's achievements in other arenas, citing its effective use of sanctions, its food and vaccine programs and its peacekeeping efforts in El Salvador, Namibia and Cambodia.
He said the dangers of the cold-war era had been replaced by new threats of terrorism and ethnic nationalism, international crime, drug traffickers, famine and disease. "So my friends, in this increasingly interdependent world, we have more common opportunities and more common enemies than ever before," he said.
Mr. Clinton spoke before a huge Presidential seal projected onto the Opera House stage, while the morning's other speakers stood under a projection of the blue-and-white logo of the United Nations.
The California design company hired to stage the ceremony said it had chosen to put the seal on the backdrop because displaying it on the lectern would have distracted attention from the United Nations Charter mounted there.
Ginny Terzano, a White House spokeswoman, said Mr. Clinton had no reluctance about being photographed standing beneath a United Nations symbol. "It was quite a surprise to us," she said of the arrangement. "We didn't ask for it."

HEADLINE: Professor Will Step Down As Head of Black Studies

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

 BODY:
In a quiet end to his troubled chairmanship of City College's Black Studies Department, Prof. Leonard Jeffries will step down at the end of this month, college officials said last night. Earlier in the evening, the City University of New York's board of trustees approved the department's choice of a new chairman, described by colleagues as a political scientist with moderate views.
Dr. Jeffries, the fiery speaker whose anti-Semitic remarks polarized the campus and spurred a three-year legal effort by the college to strip him of his chairmanship, did not seek to renew his three-year appointment, which expired this year, Dr. Yolanda T. Moses, the president of City College, said. The eight-member faculty of the Black Studies Department voted unanimously to elect a new chairman. 
 The new department chairman is Moyibi J. Amoda, originally of Nigeria, who has been at the college and in the department since 1971.
Professor Jeffries, who will remain a tenured professor, could not be reached for comment last night. Some faculty members outside the department speculated that the college administration -- emboldened by a Federal appeals court ruling in April that upheld the college's rights in removing Professor Jeffries because of his remarks -- made it clear to the Black Studies Department that it would not tolerate Professor Jeffries's reappointment. But Dr. Moses denied any such pressure.
"Clearly, there was some determination internally that there was going to be a new chair," she said. "It wasn't done from outside at all."
Dr. Moses said she was pleased with the department's action, which she characterized as a firm decision to depart from Professor Jeffries's leadership of more than two decades. "They decided it was time to focus on the students and on the department, and a new person would lead in that direction," she said.
Dr. Amoda is a specialist in African politics who has written texts on political theory and economic development. The professor, who was in Nigeria and could not be reached for comment, briefly served as acting chairman of the Black Studies Department during the 1971-72 academic year.
Dr. Amoda, a 1965 summa cum laude graduate of Dartmouth College, received his master's and doctorate degrees from the University of California, Berkeley.
Dr. Moses, and a few faculty members who were reached last night, emphasized that Dr. Amoda was independent-minded and seemed to have stayed clear of interdepartmental politics during Professor Jeffries's long tenure.
"On those occasions the topic of Dr. Jeffries has come up, he has been extremely circumspect," said Bernard Sohmer, a mathematics professor, whom Dr. Jeffries called "the head Jew at City College" in 1991 remarks at a black arts and cultural festival that first brought him widespread attention.
Dr. Sohmer said that Dr. Amoda's views -- which he described as more mainstream than Dr. Jeffries's Afrocentric emphasis -- would bolster the department's academic reputation. Ideologically, Dr. Amoda seems to espouse the reverse of Professor Jeffries's racially separatist views, Professor Sohmer said.
"He's politically left of center," he said. "He thinks there should be a community of nations with less sovereignty and more cooperation."
It is not clear if the new chairman will have the political support to steer the Black Studies Department in a new direction -- one that Dr. Moses said she hoped would be less political and more scholarly.
Although Dr. Amoda garnered unanimous support during the election, there was some speculation last night about whether the department's faculty made that choice out of heartfelt conviction or because it was felt that to continue supporting Professor Jeffries was a lost cause. "I genuinely don't know if he can take this department into a new direction," said Jeffrey Rosen, the dean of social sciences, which includes the Black Studies Department.
When the college briefly installed a new chairman, Dr. Edmund W. Gordon, in the department in 1992it was widely acknowledged that loyalty to Dr. Jeffries had paralyzed Dr. Gordon's efforts to spark change.
Reached last night, however, Dr. Gordon, who is still on the faculty, sounded hopeful about the department's future. He said: "I would think that his decision not to run for the chairmanship means he will probably not interfere with Amoda and that the faculty is free to move in another direction."
After City College stripped Professor Jeffries of his department chairmanship in 1992, a Federal court jury in Manhattan found that the institution had violated his right to free speech. A Federal appeals court upheld the verdict in 1994, resulting in his reinstatement. But three months ago, the court reversed its own decision.

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY DAILY BRIEFING;
Hospitals to Get State Subsidies

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman signed legislation yesterday to give $475 million in subsidies to all hospitals in the state to help offset their costs for treating poor patients who are not covered by Medicaid or Medicare. But a new source of money must be found by next year or aid to hospitals will end, Health Commissioner Len Fishman told The Associated Press. The "charity care" reimbursement program has cost $1.5 billion over three years. The money has been raised by diverting tax dollars allocated to the unemployment trust fund, a source that may no longer be available.

HEADLINE: A Correction: Market Timing

 BODY:

Today
 
The Japanese Government will announce further details of its economic stimulus package. It is expected to tackle issues like the weak Tokyo stock market and unemployment. Stock traders say that if the package turns out to be a disappointment, it could lead to heavy selling on the Tokyo Stock Exchange. 

The Conference Board will release its consumer confidence index for June. Forecast: 102.2, up from 101.6 in May, but down from 104.6 in April.
 
Orange Country votes on referendum to raise its sales tax a half-cent, a move that analysts said would help get the county out of bankruptcy by raising $130 million a year.
 
General Mills to report fourth-quarter earnings. Forecast: 37 cents a share.
 
Thursday
 
The United States is scheduled to put into effect tariffs on $5.9 billion worth of Japanese luxury car imports. The Japanese have said that they will then impose their own tariffs on American goods. The Japanese are said to be considering American products like cars and car parts, aircraft and food as targets for sanctions.
 
The Commerce Department will report new home sales for May. Forecast: up 3.4 percent to 600,000, up from a 2.7 percent decline in April.
 
Friday
 
The Commerce Department will issue the final revision to first-quarter gross domestic product. Forecast: a 3.1 percent increase, up from the initial revision in May of 2.7 percent.
 
The Commerce Department will also report on May factory orders. They fell 2.1 percent in April and were down 0.4 percent in March.

HEADLINE: Congressional Proposal Adds Element of Risk to Sugar-Growing Business

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO 

DATELINE: BELLE GLADE, Fla.

 BODY:
When Paul R. Orsenigo, a vegetable farmer, decided to diversify last year and grow sugar cane, it seemed a sure bet. Cane is not only a hardier crop than leaf lettuce or parsley, he reasoned, it is also money in the bank because the Federal Government guarantees its price.
"Vegetable farming is a colossal gamble," Mr. Orsenig said recently as he drove through his 320-acre farm outside this town on the shores of Lake Okeechobee, 69 miles northwest of Miami. "Sugar cane helps you weather some of the bad years you have in vegetables." 
 But Mr. Orsenigo, 37, made his plans just in time to see the stability of sugar prices threatened by a fiscally conservative Congress, which is reviewing all farm price supports that were inaugurated during the New Deal in an effort to restore rural prosperity. Nearly 100 Representatives now support a House bill that would eliminate the Federal program of crop loans that guarantees participating sugar growers and processors a minimum price. That price has usually been about twice the price paid on the world market, though price supports for crops American farmers export are more in line with market prices.
To prop up the sugar price, the Government limits sugar imports and domestic production. A 1993 report by the General Accounting Office said that while the import limits insulated producers from lower world prices, the inflated domestic price costs American consumers and manufacturers of sweetened products $1.4 billion a year.
Known for its powerful lobby, the sugar industry, which is dominated by a limited number of corporations, has benefited from some form of Government intervention for decades and from the current price-support program since 1981. The program not only helps growers of cane and sugar beets but also indirectly allows their primary competitors, corn syrup manufacturers, to charge more and still be competitive.
That is why corporate giants like Coca-Cola and Hershey Foods Corporation have joined forces to fight the program, which they see as forcing up their cost of doing business, with such unlikely allies as the Audubon Society and the Everglades Coalition, which have long blamed fertilizer-laden runoff from sugar farms for encouraging a rampant growth of vegetation that chokes Florida's wetlands.
Environmentalists hope that eliminating the sugar loans would slow the growth of sugar fields, even shrink them, and accelerate restoration of the Everglades by allowing the Government to buy land sugar production has been claiming and return it to marsh wetlands and water retention areas.
Opponents of the sugar program argue that more than helping small farmers, it enriches a few wealthy growers -- at the expense of the environment and consumers big and small.
To growers like Mr. Orsenigo, a small farmer, the program offers a reasonable sugar price and jobs. To legislators bent on change, it is held up as one of the most egregious examples of corporate welfare.
"Production caps, special loans, import protections -- all of these are things very hard to justify these days when you're trying to balance the budget," said Representative Porter J. Goss, Republican of Florida, who voted for the program when it came up for renewal in 1990 but warned the industry then to begin planning for its demise. "This is not just about sugar but about Government involvement in the marketplace."
The debate is fiercest here in Florida, where more than half the nation's sugar cane is produced and where two growers, the United States Sugar Corporation and Flo-Sun Inc., account for two-thirds of that production. They are among the 1 percent of sugar cane farms that get 58 percent of the sugar program's benefits, the accounting office found.
Possible loss of the loan program is only one of several problems the industry has confronted lately. It has been criticized for its labor practices with migrant cane cutters, for its generous campaign contributions to both Republicans and Democrats and for its reluctance to clean up its pollution. In the recent settlement of a Federal lawsuit against the State of Florida, sugar growers in Florida agreed to pay $320 million in restoration and clean-up expenses in the Everglades, with taxpayers paying the rest of the $700 million costs.
The industry employs about 14,400 people in Florida, and 50,000 to 70,000 nationwide, but claims hundreds of thousands of indirect jobs in 42 states. In Clewiston, headquarters of U.S. Sugar, the five-month harvest season that begins each November brings with it the smell of molasses and cash.
"U.S. Sugar owns the town," said Barbara Fricke, an owner of Floyd's, a department store just outside Clewiston. "When U.S. Sugar has problems, there is less money in the town, and that's less money for us."
Sugar is the third-largest cash crop in Florida, after citrus and tomatoes, accounting for about 10 percent of the state's yearly farm revenue of $5 billion to $6 billion, officials said. Gov. Lawton Chiles, a Democrat, supports the Federal sugar program, as does Florida's Commissioner of Agriculture, Bob Crawford, who argues that it would be "economically suicidal" for the nation to remove protections while other nations continue to subsidize their sugar growers.
Sugar companies contend that without the loan program, many farmers would be wiped out, unable to ride out the price fluctuations and that imports would then flood the market because the United State would no longer have a bargaining chip to use as an incentive to other countries to influence them to drop their own subsidies.
More than 100 countries produce sugar, and all have some form of Government intervention in the market to insure low prices for domestic consumption, Federal agriculture officials said. Only about 13 percent of the sugar consumed in this country comes from abroad now.
"You can't get them to make that kind of adjustment in agriculture if they believe that you will unilaterally disarm in certain industries," Jorge Dominicis, a vice president with Flo-Sun, Florida's largest sugar cane grower with 180,000 acres, said of foreign competitors.
But opponents of the sugar program point at Flo-Sun, owned by two brothers, Alfonso and Jose Fanjul of Palm Beach, as the best example of how the sugar program works to enrich a few. The G.A.O. estimated the brothers received $64 million in additional revenue in 1991 because of it. Company spokesmen, however, said the figures were calculated using world market prices without figuring in national subsidies that help bring them down.
The industry also argues that the price supports exist at no net cost to the Government because the program is not a direct subsidy. And its officials scoff at the idea that sweetener users like Coca-Cola would pass on their savings to average consumers.
But critics like Representative Dan Miller, Republican of Florida, co-sponsor of the bill on crop supports, see the program as an anachronism that makes no economic sense. He said he envisioned a free market where efficient farmers will thrive and sugar prices would drop somewhere from the 22 cents a pound currently charged here to the 14 cents sugar commands in the world market. He added that he did not expect passage of the bill to cause production to decline much or have any cataclysmic change.
Sugar growers say they doubt their foes will succeed because of the potential political cost to legislators from sugar districts. Only 5 of 23 Florida Representatives have rallied behind the House bill so far. In addition to Florida, sugar cane is grown in Hawaii, Louisiana, Texas and Puerto Rico; sugar beets are grown in 14 states. Growers of sugar beets are also mobilizing to oppose the move to cut crop supports.
Representative Mark Foley, Republican of Florida and an ally of the sugar industry, said recently he feared a cycle in which "these other nations will have us hostage and we'll pay higher prices for a product we don't grow ourselves." Members of Congress such as Mr. Goss say they expect a phasing out over enough time to give the industry a chance to reconfigure itself or "grow something else."
Meanwhile, the realization that life as they know it might change is beginning to sink in here. Steve Weeks, Mayor of Belle Glade, with a population of 18,000, said the city has been trying to diversify, looking beyond agriculture. Already there are plans for an industrial park to attract businesses and a marina on Lake Okeechobee to draw tourists.
"We're in a great place. There are a lot of good ideas," said the Mayor, a Belle Glade native who says his own lumber yard business is at stake. "It's just going to take time."

GRAPHIC: Photo: After taking up growing sugar cane to cushion his farm against the ups and downs of the vegetable market, Paul Orsenigo is worried that a move against price supports in Congress will upset the conditions he was counting on when he made his plans. (Jennifer Podis for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Securities Litigation Measure Nears Passage in the Senate

BYLINE: By NEIL A. LEWIS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The Senate tonight moved swiftly toward a final vote on legislation that would make it far more difficult for investors to sue corporations and stockbrokers for securities fraud.
Opponents of the measure tried repeatedly today either to slow the bill or to modify its effects but were beaten back at every turn. The legislation is expected to pass as early as Wednesday morning with support from both Democrats and Republicans. 
 The House has already passed a similar measure. The Clinton Administration has not yet taken an explicit position but has suggested that some parts of the legislation are necessary.
One principal area of contention over the last several days of debate has been provisions that would shield corporate officers, and others who make predictions about a company's prospects, from stockholder fraud suits when their assessments prove unfounded and the stock price drops precipitously as a result. The bill would provide a "safe harbor," in which those who made such predictions could not be held liable under most circumstances.
The measure's opponents, chiefly Senator Paul S. Sarbanes, Democrat of Maryland, argued that allowing company executives, stockbrokers and accountants, among others, to make such statements free of liability would increase corporate irresponsibility. Mr. Sarbanes said small investors who had been deceived would be left with little recourse.
But supporters of the bill like Senator Pete V. Domenici, Republican of New Mexico, said that at present the fear of being sued prevented corporate officers from making predictions that would be useful for investors.
After lengthy debate, the Senate beat back two Sarbanes-sponsored amendments that would have changed the safe-haror provisions of the measure. The main amendment would have deleted the safe-harbor provisions entirely, instead leaving the task to the Securities and Exchange Commission. Mr. Sarbanes said the matter was too technical to be included in legislation and should rather be left to the S.E.C. to deal with in regulations.
Arthur M. Levitt, the S.E.C. chairman, has objected to the safe-harbor concept in the House and Senate bills alike and has asked that the matter be left to his agency. Nonetheless, the Senate easily defeated Mr. Sarbanes's proposal, 56 to 43.

GRAPHIC: Photo: If all goes as planned, the Senate today will pass legislation that would make it much harder for stockholders to sue companies in which they invest. The bill's chief sponsor, Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato of New York, declared yesterday that frivolous lawsuits were "making it difficult for companies to raise the capital needed to fuel our economy." (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Panel Votes to Keep Tobacco Subsidies

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Led by a bipartisan bloc of Southern representatives, the House Appropriations Committee today defeated an attempt to end the 50-year-old Federal Government subsidy to tobacco growers.
But in another move that cut as deeply across party lines, the committee by a surprisingly wide margin approved an amendment to extend a moratorium on new offshore oil drilling. 
 That vote reversed a subcommittee decision to leave the extension out of the legislation, which would have allowed the 13-year-old moratorium to end. The ban applies to much of the Pacific Coast, the Florida Gulf Coast, and Alaskan and New England coastal waters; the delegations of those states led the effort to extend it.
"The risks and potential hazards to the environment outweighed the economic benefits of new explorations," said Representative Frank Riggs, a Republican from California.
The committee, which has 32 Republicans and 24 Democrats, held firm on scores of cuts in Federal spending on science, the environment, and arts and humanities programs.
By unanimous voice votes, the committee approved appropriations bills that cover the Interior and Agriculture departments.
The full House of Representatives and the Senate must still vote on the appropriations bills, and losers in both fights vowed that they would find the means to bring the issues back up again.
Legislation within the Interior bill would eliminate the Bureau of Mines, the agency responsible for mine safety; make a 40 percent cut in energy-conservation programs, and strip the National Park Service of most of its money for land acquisition.
This bill would also cut the appropriation to the National Endowment for the Arts to $82 million from $134 million.
The tobacco-subsidy amendment failed on a vote of 17 yeas and 30 nays during the committee's consideration of the Agriculture appropriations bill for 1996.
The debate began like a whisper but quickly rose to a storm.
"Mr. Chairman, I have an amendment, and it is controversial," said Representative Richard J. Durbin of Illinois, sponsor of the tobacco amendment and the ranking Democrat on the Appropriations subcommittee on agriculture.
Mr. Durbin's statement brought laughs from some committee members, and grumblings from others who represent tobacco-growing states.
"Tobacco is unlike any other crop subsidized by the United States Department of Agriculture," Mr. Durbin said. "Tobacco, when used as directed, will kill you.
"Why, in God's name, when we are talking about cutting Medicaid and education, are we still subsidizing the production and the manufacture of tobacco?"
By Washington standards, the Federal money that tobacco farmers receive is tiny -- currently $49 million, Mr. Durbin said, and much of that is offset by nearly $33 million in annual assessments that the Government collects from tobacco growers.
But the fight over tobacco portends an even bigger battle over Government subsidies for sugar, peanuts and other agricultural products. Representatives from agricultural-based states were quick to mount defenses today.
"It's an economic argument we are having here," said Representative W. G. (Bill) Hefner, Democrat of North Carolina. "It's not going to stop anybody from smoking or taking up the habit."
Representative Harold Rogers, Republican of Kentucky, provided the crossfire.
"If you adopt this amendment, it is the small farmer who you are going to hurt," he said. "We are not here talking about a health issue today. We are talking about whether small farmers are going to be able to grow tobacco."
The fight to extend the ban on drilling was driven largely by environmentalists fearful of oil spills and landowners who objected to more offshore rigs, which they feel would degrade their seaside vistas and devalue their property.
All 23 members of the Florida delegation and 28 of the 54 Californians in the House support the ban, as do the Governors of California, Florida and Alaska. Together they form a political front that is likely to withstand any attacks on the House floor.
"We've never busted that bloc before," said Robert L. Livingston, Republican of Louisiana, chairman of the Appropriations Committee, who temporarily surrendered his gavel to lead the fight against extending the moratorium. .
But he was not ready to concede defeat.
"We may take it up on the floor," he told reporters after the vote. "We have to take a look at the numbers."

Forbes, Republican of New York, at the House Appropriations Committee, which decided yesterday to save a subsidy for tobacco growers and extend a moratorium on new offshore oil drilling. ( Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: With Money Threatened, Colleges Are Moving on All Lobbying Fronts

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
When Nannerl O. Keohane, the president of Duke University, recently visited Representative Richard A. Gephardt to seek his help in warding off proposed cuts in Federal support for university research, she was careful to say something nice about Matt.
Matt, who graduated from Duke two years ago, is the oldest son of Mr. Gephardt, the House minority leader. 
 It was a small thing. While the debate in Congress over Federal patronage for university research is sure to address great issues of social policy, like most Congressional legislation, it will be shaped by things both great and small, perhaps even where an important Congressman's son went to college.
Ms. Keohane's purpose in visiting Mr. Gephardt, a Missouri Democrat, as in visits with other legislators, was to come to grips with what the president of Harvard, Neil L. Rudenstine, has called "the most hazardous moment" for Federal support for university research since World War II. Congressional budget cutters have proposed drastic reductions in the $11 billion in Federal money that goes to university research on everything from cancer treatments to cultivating better tomatoes.
 
More Than Money Is at Stake
 The money is not only important for the results, but for the universities' prestige and finances. The grants may include the salaries of the principal investigator as well as laboratory supplies and equipment and such indirect costs as heating and lighting.
Duke's share of the Federal research pie is $176 million a year, or about a fifth of the university's total budget. Johns Hopkins University, which receives the biggest share, is getting $674 million. M.I.T. receives about $267 million, Stanford about $264 million and Harvard $182 million.
With the 1996 Federal budget now in the hands of the authorizing and appropriating committees of the House of Representatives, university chancellors and presidents like Ms. Keohane have been flocking to Washington as lobbyists.
Some of the efforts of these academic salesmen appear to have paid off. For example, Charles Vest, the soft-spoken president of M.I.T., who has become the unofficial spokesman for research universities in Washington, recently met with Speaker Newt Gingrich to push for Federal support for scientific research.
The meeting was scheduled for 15 minutes, one of Mr. Vest's aides said, but stretched to an hour and a half at Mr. Gingrich's request. About a week later, Mr. Gingrich appeared on the NBC News program "This Week" and declared that he had asked all House Republican committee and subcommittee chairmen "to find every possible way to maximize the money that goes into research and development because I am concerned that we're going to cut too deeply into science."
 
Early Success In a Long Fight
 A number of college presidents, acting at the urging of the American Council on Education, an umbrella group representing 1,600 colleges, recently swarmed over Capitol Hill to campaign for passage of an amendment to the Senate budget resolution that would reduce a proposed $9.4 billion cut in Federal student loan programs over seven years to $4.4 billion. The amendment passed.
Senator Paul Simon, the Illinois Democrat who co-sponsored the amendment, singled out the council for praise, saying it "set the stage for passage."
But the budget battle in Washington is far from over, and what appear to be early victories for universities can unravel over the course of the budget fight. A strong-minded appropriations subcommittee, which decides the actual dollar amount to be spent on specific programs, may decide to ignore the decisions of the authorizing subcommittee, which provides broad authority for Government programs and sets overall spending limits.
Further, university lobbyists must cope with the diffuse sources of scientific research money. Most of the $11 billion is channeled through the six agencies. These six, with 1993 research amounts (the most recent available), are the National Institutes of Health ($5.4 billion), the National Science Foundation ($1.6 billion), the Department of Defense ($1.6 billion), the National Aeronautics and Space Administration ($631 million), the Department of Energy ($582 million) and the Department of Agriculture ($462 million).
 
Lacking Muscles, They Work on Egos
 Compared with the lobbies of the National Association of Manufacturers and the United Automobile Workers, among the boldest and well-financed groups, the university lobbyists are meek, perpetually hard-pressed financially and frequently disorganized.
Yet college and university leaders are not without significant means for encouraging members of Congress to accede to their wishes, including conferring honorary degrees, sharing valuable survey data that their political scientists have collected and, above all, providing prestigious speaking platforms.
Cornelius Pings, president of the American Association of Universities, among the first line of university lobbying groups, said that what he calls "the trained-minds argument" makes the strongest possible case for university research.
"We're ever more in a society based on information and knowledge, which allows us to compete in a world economy," he said. "The universities do the training for that."
Mr. Vest of M.I.T. said that in his talks with members of the Massachusetts Congressional delegation he frequently cited a study done a few years ago by the Bank of Boston on the economic impact that M.I.T. had in Massachusetts. It showed that no fewer than 300,000 people were employed in the state by companies utilizing the fruits of research conducted at M.I.T. "That usually elicits some real recognition," Mr. Vest said.
 
Biggest Universities Have Own Lobbyists
 Because many colleges and universities have come to believe that the national higher-education organizations cannot help them keep in touch with their home-state Congressional delegations as well as they can do the job themselves, a number have established part-time or full-time offices in Washington, thus creating a second layer in the higher-education lobby.
The University of California established one of the first permanent offices here in 1966, although the greatest growth of permanent university lobbyists in Washington took place in the 1980's. In that time about a dozen universities, including Harvard, M.I.T., Princeton and Duke, have opened offices in Washington. Most of them employ several people.
Also effective in helping preserve university research money are professional organizations like the 42,000-member Federation of American Societies for Experimental Biology, known as Faseb. After the Senate Budget Committee recently proposed cutting $8 billion from the National Institutes of Health, lobbyists for the biologists' group and similar professional associations demonstrated how effective they could be.
Mark O. Hatfield, the Oregon Republican who is chairman of the Senate Appropriations Committee, said their lobbying greatly assisted him in mustering a vote of 85 to 14 to restore $7 billion to the National Institutes of Health budget.
"Faseb, the endocrinologists and the immunologists not only wrote letters," he said. "They were here on the Hill presenting themselves in person. They were vital to our success."
 
Personal Relations Part of Process
 Experienced Washington lobbyists say that in addition to political and philosophical considerations, legislation is invariably a mosaic of human relationships.
For example, Francis L. Lawrence, the president of Rutgers University, likes to joke with Rodney Frelinghuysen, a freshman Republican from New Jersey who serves on the House Appropriations Committee.
Over the years, Mr. Frelinghuysen has taken a lot of ribbing for having broken with a long-standing family tradition and gone to Hobart College instead of Rutgers.
Someone who knows both men said that a frown from Mr. Lawrence suggesting, "Oh, you're not going to let us down again!" is enough to make Representative Frelinghuysen crack a smile.
The friendship of Alfred F. Hurley, president of the University of North Texas, with Representative Dick Armey, the Texas Republican and majority leader, dates from the 1970's, when Mr. Armey taught economics at the university.
Two years ago, when Mr. Hurley feared that Pentagon budget cutters would shut down the campus Reserve Officers Training Corps unit, he appealed to Mr. Armey for help.
Mr. Armey said he would do what he could, Mr. Hurley recalled, but he said Mr. Armey added with a laugh, "Don't let anybody at the Pentagon know you're a friend of mine!"
The R.O.T.C. unit was spared. James J. Duderstadt, president of the University of Michigan at Ann Arbor and a former nuclear physicist, said he had an excellent rapport with Representative Vernon J. Ehlers, a Michigan Republican who said he was the first research physicist to be elected to Congress.
The feeling is mutual, Mr. Ehlers said. "When we're in a committee meeting discussing an issue like funding for nuclear accelerators," he said, "usually Jim and I are the only two people in the room who know what we're talking about."
George Rupp, the president of Columbia University, said he had personally lobbied all four members of the Senate who are Columbia graduates. He noted he was particularly encouraged by his relationship with Senator Judd Gregg, class of '69, a conservative Republican from New Hampshire and a member of the Senate Budget Committee.
"He's not imperial like some, but down to earth," Senator Gregg said of Mr. Rupp. He added that although he remained "totally committed" to balancing the Federal budget, after his conversations with Mr. Rupp, he intended to go easy on the university research budgets.

HEADLINE: CASTRO CONFERS WITH EXILED FOE

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO 

DATELINE: MIAMI, June 27

 BODY:
Fidel Castro said, "You've let your hair grow long."
Eloy Gutierrez Menoyo said, "It's not my style, but my wife likes it long, so long it is." 
 With that, and a handshake, two adversaries who last saw each other 30 years ago, when Mr. Castro put Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo in prison for 22 years, sat down to talk about Cuba and its future. They met for more than three hours last week in Havana, the first time Mr. Castro has received an opposition exile leader.
Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo, a guerrilla commander in the Cuban revolution who now heads Cambio Cubano (Cuban Change), an organization that advocates a peaceful transition to democracy in Cuba, said that the granting of a meeting he had sought for the last two years demonstrated Mr. Castro's desire for political change. But experts on Cuban affairs, including some Clinton Administration officials, said it remained to be seen whether it was a significant overture.
In recounting his exchange with Mr. Castro in an interview at his home here on Monday, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo refused to disclose details of their conversation. But he said he had asked the Cuban President to be allowed to take his exile organization to Cuba as an opposition party.
In a statement in which he defended his decision to repatriate Cubans who flee the island on boats and rafts, President Clinton today reaffirmed his support for the American trade embargo against Cuba but said "we are prepared to respond with our own carefully calibrated response" if Cuba takes steps to institute "far-reaching political and economic reform."
A senior Administration official said Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo's visit had yet to prove to be something more than an "individual interaction" and did not constitute a meaningful change under the President's policy.
"What we're looking for is something more institutional than a meeting," said the official, who spoke on condition that he not be identified. "If Cambio Cubano were allowed to return and hold meetings and exercise their political rights, that's something that would be a meaningful change that we should recognize."
In the interview, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo said he had expressed to Mr. Castro his wish to move to Cuba and for all Cubans to "have the right to return to their homeland."
"That we could finish our conversation in a civilized way and leave the door open is very important," said Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo, who did not get any promises but said he found Mr. Castro "receptive and tolerant."
"Everything will be studied and everything will be taken into account," he said. "I exposed my arguments and I hope that he will analyze them and answer eventually."
Jose Ponce, first secretary of the Cuban interests section in Washington, said the meeting was "another sign of our flexibility" and willingness to deal with Cuban exiles who respected Cuba's sovereignty in trying to address its problems.
The meeting was a face-to-face between two longtime foes who once were allies in the victorious struggle to overthrow the dictatorship of Fulgencio Batista. Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo, 60, broke with Mr. Castro in 1961 over his refusal to hold elections and returned three years later to organize a rebellion. He was captured and stayed in prison until 1986, when Prime Minister Felipe Gonzalez of Spain intervened to win his release.
Two years ago, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo publicly renounced violence as a tool for political change and helped form Cambio Cubano to call for a peaceful transfer of power in Cuba.
Although respected for his political imprisonment, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo is belittled by conservative Cuban exiles who advocate a hard line, including a tightening of the economic embargo and a naval blockade, to bring Mr. Castro's Government down. In Spanish-language radio here, his visit to Cuba has been the theme of talk shows for days, with callers labeling him "traitor," "shameless," "less of a man" and "Spanish, not Cuban." He was born in Spain but moved with his family to Cuba when he was 11.
"People have given it importance because exiles are desperate for solutions," said Jose Miro Torra, head of Brigade 2506, a group of veterans of the 1961 Bay of Pigs invasion. "But the visit is worth nothing."
While Cambio Cubano is small, with only a few hundred adherents, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo is part of a broad spectrum of views in the exile community on what to do to quicken the pace of change in Cuba. Polls consistently show an openness among many Cuban exiles to consider a variety of options to deal with the island.
For Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo, it was a sign of new tolerance that a few hundred supporters rather than protesters greeted him at the airport here upon his return from Cuba on Sunday. Only last year, a delegation that included his daughter, Patricia, was harassed and threatened by irate Cuban exiles after attending a reception in which they shook hands, kissed or chatted amiably with Mr. Castro.
Still, even those who lauded Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo were cautious, saying Mr. Castro had given no concrete signs that he was ready for a significant move toward democracy. Ramon Cernuda, an exile leader, noted that he, Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo and a Miami lawyer, Alfredo Duran, met with the Cuban Foreign Minister, Roberto Robaina, nine months ago in Madrid in what they thought would be the first of many more meetings.
"Nothing happened," Mr. Cernuda said. "We can't fall on the trap of mirages."
Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo said that during his 10-day visit to Cuba he discussed civil liberties, dissidents and political prisoners with Mr. Castro and Mr. Robaina, who also attended the meeting in a private office at Havana's convention center.
The exile leader said that his movements were not restricted and that he talked with dissidents, foreign reporters and former guerrilla comrades in arms from the city of Cienfuegos. He said his visit was briefly mentioned by both Cuban television and the state-owned newspaper, Granma.
Mr. Gutierrez Menoyo left for Mexico today at the invitation of President Ernesto Zedillo. It is the start of a campaign, he said, to try to drum up support for discussions with Cuba to overcome the hurdles to free elections. He said he trusted that the United States would eventually join in the effort, if only to avoid a crisis on the island that would unleash another wave of refugees.
"There is a road full of obstacles ahead of us and what is important is to reach an agreement," he said.
Of his visit, he said: "Castro may gain by improving his image but Cambio Cubano gains enormously. We are contributing to create a hope for change, and once that gets started in a country, there's nothing that can stop it."

HEADLINE: DIARY;
Yesterday in Washington

 BODY:

ENVIRONMENTAL REFORM -- Senators battling Federal regulations are aiming at what would seem to be one of the least burdensome but most effective of environmental rules: requiring manufacturers to publicly disclose how much they pollute. At the urging of chemical companies, a provision to scale back the program has been tucked into a regulatory relief bill that may come to the Senate floor this week.
 
ECONOMIC ADVISERS -- An effort by Republicans on the House Appropriations Subcommittee on Treasury, Postal Service and General Government to kill off the Council of Economic Advisers has drawn criticism from past council chairmen from both parties, who say it provides the President with an important independent source of economic advice. Representative Jim Ross Lightfoot of Iowa, chairman of the subcommittee, says the President had enough economists in the Office of Management and Budget and the National Economic Council. But Herbert G. Stein, a council chairman under President Richard M. Nixon, said, "I think it's a stupid idea." 

TOBACCO SUBSIDIES -- Led by Southern representatives, the House Appropriations Committee defeated an attempt to end the 50-year-old Federal Government subsidy to tobacco growers. But in another alignment that cut as deeply across party lines, the committee by a surprisingly wide margin approved an amendment to extend the moratorium on new off-shore oil drilling.
 
LEGAL OVERHAUL -- The Senate moved toward a vote on legislation that would drastically alter the way companies are regulated by making it more difficult to sue corporations and stockbrokers when share prices drop suddenly. The bill is expected to pass as early as today with support from Democrats and Republicans alike.

HEADLINE: TV Guide Taps Editor to Lead On-Line Unit

BYLINE: By DEIRDRE CARMODY 

 BODY:
News America Publishing yesterday named Barry Golson, executive editor of TV Guide, as editor in chief of a new unit for magazine development and new media.
Among his duties, Mr. Golson will oversee TV Guide Online, an electronic version of the magazine's television listings scheduled to go on the Internet in September. 
 Mr. Golson, 50, said he would also be looking at publishing possibilities, including CD-ROM and print, but declined to be more specific.
"You can assume that a CD-ROM based on TV Guide would be a natural kind of thing for us to do," he said. "You can assume that a lot of people have come to us with publishing ideas ranging from small to large. And at the same time we are going to put a total focus on bringing our biggest property on line in an interesting way."
He has already been working for some time on developing TV Guide Online. Its link to the Internet will the Delphi Internet Services Corporation, which, like News America, is a part of Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation. Last month, MCI Communications made a $2 billion investment in Delphi, joining the race to get onto the global computer network.
The urge to reinvent television listings keeps growing. The possibility that there might some day be 500 television channels made it clear two or three years ago that a small-sized magazine like TV Guide would be unable to list them all.
Now, the average cable system offers more than 12,000 program choices a week on roughly 30 channels. But viewers say that on most systems, the listings display scrolls too slowly to be of much use.
Yet another attempt to solve the problem comes from Starsight Telecast Inc. of Fremont, Calif, which recently introduced a new kind of on-screen guide that is available to owners of specially equipped TV sets, VCR's and set-top boxes.
Mr. Golson would not give any details about TV Guide Online, except to say that a "small test to test the muscles of our staff" was under way.
Mr. Golson was executive editor of Playboy from 1977 to 1989. He left to join News America as the founding editor of Men's Life, a publication whose own life lasted only one issue. It went on sale in September 1990 and quickly closed, one of many victims of the advertising recession.
A successor to Mr. Golson at TV Guide has not yet been named.
