HEADLINE: Confidence Of Consumers Falls Sharply

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Consumer confidence took its steepest fall in nearly three years in June, dropping to the lowest level since late last year, the Conference Board reported today.
The surprisingly big decline reported by the Conference Board, a New-York based business research group, was taken as a sign that consumers, their incomes growing slowly and saddled with high debt, will continue to spend cautiously in coming months. 
 Still, analysts tended to play down the effect of this latest economic reading on the Federal Reserve, which many believe is likely to lower short-term interest rates at a meeting next week.
They said that in recent years confidence had been more a mirror of current conditions than a predicting, or leading, indicator. "Confidence is sort of coming into line" with flat sales early this year, said Bruce Steinberg, a senior economist at Merrill Lynch. He added that consumer spending, which accounts for about two-thirds of economic activity, "is not going to come roaring back."
The Conference Board said that its overall index skidded to 92.8 in June from 102 in May. This was the lowest since October, when the index, based on a survey of 5,000 households, stood at 89.1.
Eight of nine geographic regions -- the only exception being the eastern south-central states -- showed declines. As in May, the lowest confidence was in the Middle Atlantic region, followed by New England.
The expectations component, which seeks to gauge activity over the next six months, was especially weak, plunging to 81.3 from 93.1, on a small decline in the number of consumers who expected business to improve and a moderate increase among those who feared it would worsen. Expectations are now the lowest since November 1993.
"Despite the relatively large decline in confidence in June and other signs of a slowing in the pace of the nation's economic growth, the current confidence level has been historically associated with a reasonably strong economy," said Fabian Linden, executive director of the board's Consumer Research Center. "Still," he said, "the magnitude of the decline is somewhat disconcerting."
Today's report was somewhat at odds with preliminary June data on public confidence published by the University of Michigan last week. That report, according to subscribers, showed a rise to 92.3 from 89.8 in May, returning to about the same level as in April.
Bond prices, which tend to rise on weak economic data, rallied after the Conference Board report was released. But they retreated later in the day, weighed down by a lackluster auction of Treasury notes and a strong weekly retail sales report from Johnson Redbook.
"We still think the economy's essentially in an inventory correction" and will begin to pick up in the second half of the year, said John E. Barnds, director of business and banking analysis for NBD Bank in Detroit.
But slow income growth and concern over jobs will restrain this, he added, and he predicted that there would be relatively little benefit from home-mortgage refinancing.
Unlike in 1993, when refinancing boomed, today's relatively low long-term rates have mainly induced home owners to shift from adjustable-rate mortgages to fixed-rate mortgages. This means that relatively few people are reducing their monthly payments -- indeed, they are often higher -- and few are withdrawing equity that could spur other spending, Mr. Barnds said.
As for current business conditions, the Conference Board found people moderately less positive than in May. The June reading was 110.1, down from 115.4 last month and from the peak recent reading, in April, of 116.2.
For employment, slightly fewer respondents called jobs in the current market plentiful, while the number of those reporting jobs hard to get were unchanged. Pessimists continued to outnumber optimists by a fairly large margin on the question of future job opportunities.
The drop in overall confidence was the largest since July 1992, when the economy was clawing its way out of recession.
Geographically, the highest confidence by far, at a reading of 126.3, was recorded in the Mountain states. In second place, at 112.4, were the states of the eastern north-central region.

HEADLINE: In Muslim Cleric's Trial, a Radical Defender;
Left-Leaning Lawyer and Revolutionary Sympathizer Comes Back in the Limelight

BYLINE: By JOSEPH P. FRIED 

 BODY:
Lynne F. Stewart, counselor at law and radical at heart, strode through the Federal courthouse in Manhattan, a purple cloth tote bag crammed with legal papers dangling from her hand, a New York Mets cap on her head.
No formidable briefcase like those being borne about her. No sign of the dress-for-success standard of lawyering also abundant in the vicinity. 
 But if the round-faced woman with the quick smile looked readier for the stands at Shea Stadium than for a day's labor in this marbled arena, that notion swiftly evaporated when she entered a heavily guarded third-floor courtroom for another round as chief lawyer for the chief defendant in the biggest terrorism trial ever to come to an American court.
Ms. Stewart's client is Sheik Omar Abdel Rahman, the militant Muslim cleric charged with leading a lethal conspiracy that prosecutors say included the 1993 bombing of the World Trade Center and aborted plots to blow up the United Nations headquarters and the Hudson River tunnels.
For the past five and a half months, he and 10 Islamic co-defendants have sat in the courtroom as prosecutors have described a scheme of intrigue and violence that, they say, constituted a "war of urban terrorism" against the United States.
In the next few days, Ms. Stewart and the other defense lawyers will begin presenting their case, hoping to prove that the defendants were ensnared in a politically motivated effort by the United States to persecute Islamic radicals who did nothing more than vehemently oppose American policies in the Middle East.
"Did he disagree with U.S. policy?" Ms. Stewart asked early in the trial, speaking of Mr. Abdel Rahman. "He did. Did he conspire, seditiously or otherwise? He did not."
For the 55-year-old Ms. Stewart, the trial has meant a return to the legal limelight that in previous years saw her defending clients ranging from members of the radical, leftist group the Weather Underground to Larry Davis, the man accused of trying to kill nine police officers in a 1986 shootout. In fact, along with lawyers like William M. Kunstler and Ronald L. Kuby, she is a spirited stalwart in the left-leaning legal ranks whose members not only represent self-styled revolutionaries but also sympathize with their causes.
She sees her current client as just the sort that the radical-left bar ought to defend.
"He's being framed because of his political and religious teachings," Ms. Stewart said. That, she suggested in a recent interview, aligns him with others she has defended, people like David J. Gilbert, the Weather Underground member who was convicted in the 1981 Brink's armored car robbery in Rockland County, or Richard C. Williams, who was convicted of setting off bombs at military sites and corporate offices in the early 1980's.
For Ms. Stewart, who lives on the lower East Side and operates from a West Village office, those earlier clients were not simply professional accounts; they were revolutionaries in a society that she herself sees as desperately needing "radical surgery." Even as she argues that Mr. Abdel Rahman did not conspire in violent schemes in America, she herself says that violence and revolution are necessary to wipe out the economic and racial injustices of America's capitalist system.
"I don't believe in anarchistic violence but in directed violence," she said. "That would be violence directed at the institutions which perpetuate capitalism, racism and sexism, and at the people who are the appointed guardians of those institutions, and accompanied by popular support." Ms. Stewart declined to specify which institutions she would include in that definition.
Even Larry Davis, whose acquittal in the shootout with the nine police officers was a stunning triumph for Ms. Stewart and her co-counsel, Mr. Kunstler, was justified in using violence, she said.
"Larry Davis was a lightning rod of a case because it was at a time when a lot of black people were being assaulted and murdered by the police," she said. "I thought the Larry Davis case had captured the feelings of the third-world community in the city because here's a kid, whether you liked what he did or not, he stood up to the police."
While Ms. Stewart says that all her high-profile clients share the distinction of being revolutionaries against unjust systems or people whose cases expose those injustices, she readily acknowledges that some of her radical-left legal colleagues question whether Mr. Abdel Rahman is the kind of defendant they can feel enthusiastic about.
As an Islamic fundamentalist, she said they told her, he has long advocated the overthrow of the Egyptian Government in favor of a religious, authoritarian state that, they said, would make short shrift of far-left dissenters like herself.
"The entire left bar raised that issue," she said. "They said, 'You'd be the first to go.' " But "there's a tradeoff" she said.
"I certainly have deep sympathy for the struggle in Egypt," she said of the people who are fighting a Government they consider oppressive. The fundamentalist movement, she said, "is the only hope for change there, the one that gathers the imagination of the people, that motivates them."
Though her words are fiery, her tone is neither didactic nor missionary. In fact, the words flow in pretty much the same effervescent voice she uses to tell of the joys of taking her 9-year-old granddaughter to watch the Mets play at Shea Stadium, where "we yell, vent and carry on."
Of the World Trade Center bombing, she said, "I think that was pretty much anarchistic." She noted that Mr. Abdel Rahman denied any involvement in that bombing or in any of the other plots charged in the case, and that he insisted he was being persecuted for his strident opposition to United States policies in the Middle East.
He and his co-defendants are not accused of carrying out the World Trade Center attack for which four other men were convicted in an earlier trial. But the prosecution says that the attack was part of an overall conspiracy by the defendants to intimidate the United States into changing Middle East policies that they saw as aiding Israel and the Egyptian Government.
Recalling her own journey into radical dissatisfaction with American society, Ms. Stewart said she had grown up "just a Protestant girl from Queens," who led a sheltered youth as the daughter of schoolteachers living in an all-white neighborhood.
Among the experiences that changed her outlook were the poverty and squalor she found in Harlem when she began working there as a school librarian and teacher in the 1960's, about a decade before she went to law school at Rutgers University. "I did not know Harlem existed," she said.
"I said, 'Why are all these people living in these conditions? I wouldn't.' My answer was, 'Because they're kept here and society had an interest in keeping them here, and I was part of it and didn't want to be but wanted to change things.' "
Those who have worked with -- and against -- her use terms like "shrewd," "highly intelligent," and "hard-working" to describe Ms. Stewart, whose courtroom manner is steady, methodical, and, as she put it, "not abrasive."
"She's very proficient and is very, very prepared," said Charles E. Rose, a former Federal prosecutor in Brooklyn who faced Ms. Stewart in the 1986 bombing trial of Mr. Williams, the man convicted of setting off bombs at military and corporate sites.
Ms. Stewart, who entered the case only six weeks before the trial began in January before Judge Michael B. Mukasey, had unusually little time to prepare for Mr. Abdel Rahman's defense. The cleric from Egypt, who is blind and does not speak English, had insisted he would defend himself, but associates finally convinced him otherwise and contacted Ramsey Clark, the former United States Attorney General. Pressed by his own caseload, Ms. Stewart said, Mr. Clark brought her into the case.
Mr. Clark, who has not been at the trial much, remains a strategic adviser and handles various out-of-court matters, Ms. Stewart said. The third lawyer on the team, Abdeen Jabara, is in court daily.
To handle the long case, Ms. Stewart had to shift most of her other work to her son and law partner, Geoffrey Stewart. Her practice involves all kinds of criminal cases, she said, except child abuse. "I taught school for 11 years and see children as true innocents and have a hard time defending people accused of this," she said.
She also said she "wouldn't go out of my way" to work with Mr. Kunstler again. Some lawyers who know both of them say Ms. Stewart resentfully believes that Mr. Kunstler hogged credit for the Larry Davis acquittals. But Ms. Stewart insisted the rift stems from other disagreements over the case and said she and Mr. Kunstler remain friends. Mr. Kunstler denied seeking undue credit and praised Ms. Stewart's abilities.
Meanwhile, the demands of the Abdel Rahman trial have had an impact of another sort: they have sharply cut Ms. Stewart's attendance at Shea Stadium, where she has managed only one visit this spring, compared to one a week before the players' strike last year.
A lust for revolution and love of baseball are easily compatible, she said. "I've always said, when the revolution comes to this country, it'll be as American as apple pie and baseball," she declared cheerily.

In the highly celebrated case, the jury accepted the argument that Mr. Davis had fired in self-defense, believing that the officers were trying to kill him. In a separate trial that year, he was acquitted of in the killings of four drug dealers. In both trials, Ms. Stewart was co-counsel with William M. Kunstler.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Early Gains In Treasury Issues Lose Momentum

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The plunge in consumer confidence helped Treasury securities make early gains yesterday, but it could not overcome a lackluster note auction or signs that the economy may not be slowing enough to allow the Fed to cut interest rates.
The 30-year bond slipped 1/32 , to a price of 1141/32 for a yield of 6.54 percent, unchanged from the previous day. Intermediate notes and shorter-maturity bill rates were either down slightly or unchanged. 
 Traders attributed the early gains to a 9-point plunge in the Conference Board's June consumer confidence survey. The index fell to 92.8, its lowest level since October 1994, versus estimates of 101.5 and against May's revised 102.0.
But market gains were limited ahead of the Treasury's two-year note auction, because of investor concern over supply. Uncertainty about the outcome of next week's Federal Open Market Committee meeting, and whether a rate cut was in the wings, made buyers hesitant, turning a usually easy sale of two-year notes into a harder sell.
Traders said that as a result, market participants grew apprehensive about today's auction of new five-year notes, which is generally a harder sell than the two-year issue.
The high yield at auction yesterday was 5.69 percent, down from 6.17 percent in May, and about three basis points higher than when-issued trading on Monday. Late yesterday the five-year note was offered at a price to yield 5.90 percent.
The consumer confidence report reconfirmed for some investors that the economy was headed for a mild recession. Those expectations were dampened in the last two trading sessions by the jump in durable orders last Friday and Monday's pickup in sales of existing homes. Also, the Johnson Redbook Service's weekly retail sales report released yesterday suggested some strength and indicated that retail sales so far through June are on pace to exceed those in May by 1.5 percent.
A Fed governor, Lawrence B. Lindsey, commented to reporters before a closed-door session with economists and business executives yesterday in New York that "we're still in a period of slow to moderate growth." But he added that he did not expect the economy to contract for two consecutive quarters. A recession is often defined as two successive quarters of economic contraction.
Reacting to the confidence report, Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Securities, said: "The fact that it declined was a modest surprise; the fact that it declined to the extent that it did was a major surprise. Clearly none expected it."
Mr. Fine noted that the Michigan consumer confidence survey of the first 15 days, reported on June 16, showed a noticeable increase, while the Conference Board's survey went the other way. "The fact that this disparity has occured is not unusual for a month-to-month period," Mr. Fine said. "It would be unusual, however, if it continued."
Not only the two measures of consumer psychology but all recent economic statistics paint a mixed pictures, he said. "The consumer confidence data certainly lends credence to the view that the Fed will lower the funds rate in July, but it is far from being the decisive factor," Mr. Fine said.
Charles Roden, managing director and head of fixed-income investing at Josephthal, Lyon & Ross, said: "Over all the market has acted pretty well. considering the recent gains it's had. Investor interest is still in the five-year to 10-year maturities, but basically the lower shorter maturities."
Mr. Roden sees a large buildup of money next week because huge amounts of bonds are retiring on top of the normal interest and principal payments. "All that reinvestment money could affect the markets," he said, citing in particular "the municipal bond market, where supply of new issues is thin."
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new two-year notes:

HEADLINE: Stocks Fall in Volatile Trading, With Dow Off by 8.64

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Stocks fell in volatile trading yesterday as market players reacted to conflicting reports on the economy and worried about the direction of interest rates.
Technology shares, concentrated in the Nasdaq stock market, took the soundest beating in the market's third consecutive retreat. Those issues have been among the best performers in recent months. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average lost 8.64 points, to 4,542.61, after advancing as much as 20 points earlier in the session. Declining issues narrowly outnumbered advances on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume totaled a heavy 346.96 million shares, up from 296.72 million in the previous session.
Stocks started the session lower, resuming the downward course of the previous two sessions that had at least temporarily halted a four-month run of new highs. Again, weak overseas markets and lower bond prices helped set the sour tone.
Shortly after the market opened, however, stocks shot higher, gaining as much as 20 points on a Conference Board report showing consumer faith in the economy had unexpectedly plunged in June, with the board's index posting the biggest monthly decline in three years. Bond prices shot higher and that rally spilled over into stocks.
The markets' optimism was based on the belief that with the economy apparently weakening the Federal Reserve was more likely to lower interest rates at next week's two-day meeting of its policy-setting Open Market Committee, traders said.
"Consumer confidence dropped much more than expected, causing a rally in the bond market and encouraging those who have been betting the Fed would cut rates next week," said Alfred Goldman, director of technical market analysis at A. G. Edwards & Sons.
Bond investors like slow economic growth because it allays fears of inflation, which erodes the value of fixed-income investments.
One of yesterday's big losers was Champion International, which fell 2 5/8, to 51, after Loews said it would sell 5 million shares of the stock at $52.45.
Pepsico Inc., slipped 1 1/4, to 45 5/8, after Goldman, Sachs removed the company from a top investment list and a teamsters' strike against Pepsi appeared to widen.
Republic New York, gained 2, to 54 3/8, after Standard & Poor's said it would add the bank holding company to its S. & P. 500-stock index after the close of trading July 3.
Club Med gained 2 3/4, to 31 7/8. A board committee recommended that shareholders accept Club Mediterranee S.A.'s new cash offer of $32 a share. The suitor currently holds 70.8 percent of Club Med's shares.
On the Amex Home Oil rose 2 7/8, to 13, on news that Amoco's Canadian Petroleum Company unit was to make a cash offer to buy all common shares of the company.
Technology issues were pounded, with Intel losing 2 3/8, to 62 5/8, in Nasdaq trading, and I.B.M. tumbling 2 7/8, to 95 3/8, on the Big Board.
However, Softkey International was up 1 5/8, to 30 3/4. A public offering of 2.3 million common shares was priced at $28.875.
Technology stocks have risen 32.7 percent since January and 65 percent over the last year, according to calculations by Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at the First Albany Corporation. Semiconductor issues, meanwhile, are up 101 percent over six months, he said.
"When you have that kind of performance, it's only common sense to expect some kind of meaningful pullback," Mr. Johnson said.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Copper Prices Fall as Inventory Shows Slower Pace of Decrease

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Copper prices tumbled yesterday on the Commodity Exchange of New York amid signs that world supply was increasing.
On other markets, corn prices fell while crude oil rose. The Commodity Research Bureau index dropped 0.62 point, to 234.56. 
 Copper prices had been moving higher as falling stockpiles in the United States and around the world indicated demand from industrial consumers like auto makers and appliance manufacturers was continuing to outstrip new supply despite signs of economic slowdowns.
While copper inventories held in London Metal Exchange warehouses fell again today, dropping by 2,725 metric tons, to 175,775 tons, its lowest since December 1990, the pace of decline has slowed recently, disappointing some traders.
"Expectations were for around 4,000 to 5,000 tons" of inventory declines, said Lawrence Eagles, an analyst with the commodity brokers GNI Ltd.
Copper for July delivery on the New York exchange fell 3.55 cents, to $1.3715 a pound, its third consecutive decline after surging to a six-year high of $1.46 a pound on June 22.
Corn prices slid for a second day as rainfall drenched the Midwest, easing concern that hot, dry weather would damage this year's crop.
Up to two inches of rain fell yesterday on parts the Corn Belt from Iowa and Missouri to Ohio and forecasters are calling for more rain during the rest of the week.
"It's still very much of a weather market and conditions look great," said Don Roose, analyst with U.S. Commodities Inc. in Des Moines.
Corn for July delivery on the Chicago Board of Trade fell 3.75 cents, to $2.635 a bushel, its lowest price in more than a month.
Crude oil climbed for the third time in four days as refineries lifted their crude oil consumption to meet robust gasoline demand, drawing down stockpiles.
This week's report from the American Petroleum Institute was expected to show crude stockpiles fell by as much as three million barrels last week as the brisk refining activity continued.
"Refiners who need the barrels are coming in to buy it," said Benedetto L'Abbate, a crude oil trader at the Agip Petroleum Company in Houston.
Crude oil for August delivery on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose 13 cents, to $17.77 a barrel.
After the market closed, the institute reported that crude oil stockpiles plunged by 6.2 million barrels, or almost 2 percent, to 319 million barrels, 8.4 million barrels below a year ago.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Is Off vs. Mark and Yen As Japan Trade Deadline Nears

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar slipped against the yen and some other leading currencies yesterday as traders grew more skeptical that the United States and Japan would reach an accord on auto trade.
American and Japanese negotiators met again yesterday in Geneva in an effort to find some sort of agreement to open Japanese markets to American cars and car parts. Washington has threatened to impose 100 percent tariffs on $5.9 billion of Japanese luxury cars if no accord is reached by midnight tonight. 
 With the sanctions deadline hanging, currency traders said they were reluctant to bet on the dollar's direction against the yen. "There are too many questions that remain to make a strong bet one way or the other," said Joe Francomano of Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank in New York.
Others said they expected the dollar to benefit regardless of the outcome of the trade talks. "If the sanctions go in place, or if they strike some kind of accord, either way it's bullish for the dollar," said Kevin Lawrie, foreign exchange manager at Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh. "Either way, we're trimming the trade deficit."
In late New York trading yesterday, the dollar slipped to 84.29 yen from 84.36 yen on Monday. It was also weaker against the German mark, declining to 1.3870 marks from 1.3907 on Monday, after reports of rising prices in western Germany dampened speculation that the central bank there might lower interest rates.
A report from the German state of Bavaria said consumer prices had risen four-tenths of 1 percent in the most recent reporting month, compared with two-tenths of 1 percent the month before. Other western German states have also reported accelerating prices, and yesterday's report squelched remaining hopes of an interest-rate cut.
In other currency trading yesterday, the British pound fell against the mark for a second day amid concern that the Conservative Government could fall. The pound's decline was further spurred after a member of Prime Minister John Major's Cabinet, John Redwood, the Welsh Secretary, resigned Monday to take up Mr. Major's challenge to his opponents to contest the Conservative Party leadership.
Against the mark yesterday, the pound dropped as low as 2.1867, its lowest level since May 10, before settling at 2.1984 marks. It slipped against the dollar to $1.5826 from $1.5852.

HEADLINE: Loss of Economic Council Held Imprudent

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
An effort by House Republicans to abolish the Council of Economic Advisers has drawn criticism from past council chairmen from both parties, who say it provides the President with an important independent source of economic advice.
A proposal to cut funds for the council's operations will be considered on Wednesday by the House Appropriations Subcommittee on the Treasury, Postal Service and General Government. Representative Jim Ross Lightfoot, the Iowa Republican who is chairman of the subcommittee, said last week that even without the council the President would have enough economists in the Office of Management and Budget and the National Economic Council. 
 Laura D'Andrea Tyson, President Clinton's first chairwoman of the Council of Economic Advisers and now chairwoman of the National Economic Council, said Representative Lightfoot "has a notion that is not based on the reality of what is going on in government."
She said the Council of Economic Advisers, which was established in 1947, provided objective economic analysis, and had a role that was different from either the budget office or the National Economic Council at the White House.
"None of these people is underutilized," she said. "Everyone is working more than full time by any reasonable characterization of hours in a day."
The Council of Economic Advisers often looks at proposals for government action and "points out that some idea will have bad consequences," she said. For example, she cited warnings from the council early in the Reagan Administration that "large deficits and high long-term interest rates were putting pressures on the American manufacturing system."
Economists at the Office of Management and Budget, she said, have another mission -- budgetary analysis. And the National Economic Council, she said, arranges the meetings and memorandums from which the President makes economic decisions.
Herbert G. Stein, chairman of the council under President Richard M. Nixon from 1972 through 1974, was even blunter. "I think it's a stupid idea," he said, adding that the council was "the only group of economists in the Government that doesn't have some agency ax to grind."
The council has a staff of about 40. "It has a budget of $3.4 million," Mr. Stein said. "The President is making decisions about a $7 trillion economy. I think we can afford to give him the best possible advice."
Mr. Stein said most other government economists promoted the interest of the department or industry to which they are connected. He said the council's advice against building a supersonic transport aircraft during the Nixon Administration "saved the country more than the whole cost of the council for 50 years."
"The main thing the council does is give the President a view of his options," Mr. Stein said. "Even if he disagrees, he makes a better decision because he has seen what his options are."
Democrats on the subcommittee plan to oppose Mr. Lightfoot's proposal, arguing that it was inappropriate to be "playing games with the White House budget," as Representative Steny H. Hoyer of Maryland put it. If the proposal is approved by the subcommittee, it could still be reversed by the full committee or the whole House, or by the Senate.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: Advertising;
Campaign by car dealer pokes fun at sales pitches, angers other dealers -- and increases his business.

BYLINE: By Anthony Ramirez 

 BODY:
CAR-DEALER advertising can be a wasteland of shrieking prices and exclamation points. But one dealer, Swanson Chrysler Plymouth in St. Petersburg, Fla., has hit on a disarming formula: poke fun at and unmask some of the most misleading practices of auto dealers.
True, venal auto dealers are an easy target. But it is a perilous strategy, nonetheless, not only because it brings the advertiser's own occupation into disrepute but also angers fellow auto dealers. The local auto dealer organization, of which Swanson Chrysler is not a member, is especially angry. 
 For the last four months, Swanson Chrysler's 11 print advertisements in local newspapers have starred a leering, potbellied cartoon character named Craven Bamboozle. "He's the kind of guy only a mother could love," reads the ad that introduced the character, "except that his mother won't speak to him since he unloaded a lemon on her (at full price)."
But instead of merely setting up a straw auto dealer, the Swanson Chrysler ads go several steps further. They detail what the dealership calls common tricks of the trade, including the "Invisible Sales Manager," the "Guaranteed Trade-In Scam," and a densely detailed ad on what it calls "leasing scams." The leasing ad even includes the address of Florida's Attorney General so consumers can report rip-offs.
So far, Swanson Chrysler's strategy has worked. A medium-size dealership, Swanson Chrysler has been hurt recently by rumors that it was about to close because of the recent illness of the owner, Cosby Swanson Jr., who is 75.
Monthly sales had dwindled before the ad campaign began in February to fewer than 50 new cars and 50 used cars. Now the ad campaign has gained the dealership enough attention so that Swanson Chrysler's sales total more than 75 new cars and 65 to 70 used cars a month, at a time when overall industry sales are slow because of the weakening national economy.
The ad campaign is the brainchild of Cosby Swanson 3d, 40, one of three sons of the elder Mr. Swanson. He is helping to run the dealership now that his father is semiretired after recovering from a stroke.
"Our ad campaign is designed not so much to tell people about cars and their prices," Mr. Swanson 3d said, "but to convince them that down the line when they are ready to buy a car they ought to take a look at our dealership."
A former law student who also studied church history at an interdenominational seminary, the younger Mr. Swanson describes himself now as an unpublished short story writer. He writes the copy for the ads and developed the character of Craven Bamboozle, a television evangelist he used to portray in skits at the seminary.
A longtime friend in North Carolina, Mark Dubowski, is the cartoonist for the ads and helps edit Mr. Swanson's copy. Mr. Dubowski, a former advertising representative at Forbes magazine, said, "The purpose of the ads is not really to poke fun at dealers but make car shopping into something that you can not only laugh about but learn something useful, too."
The car salesmen lore came straight from the source, the longtime salesmen at Swanson Chrysler who had all worked at other car dealerships. "We had about two days of skull sessions and it all came pouring out," the younger Mr. Swanson said.
Dean Silvers, the dealership's 49-year-old general manager, has been in the auto business for more than 28 years. He and other salesmen contributed tales like the Invisible Sales Manager (the one who tells the salesmen to tell the customer her offer is too low) and the Guaranteed Trade-In (the car's price is secretly increased to take into account the large trade-in).
Mr. Silvers said Florida was a special problem for older and retired people. Salesmen at one dealership, he recalled, used to fight each other when a potential customer would drive in with a tennis ball attached to the car antenna.
The reason? It meant the driver was a frail or forgetful person who had trouble finding his car in the parking lot and needed the tennis ball as a reminder.
Mr. Silvers also used to advertise cars with especially attractive low prices but especially unattractive features so no one would buy it -- such as a Thunderbird priced $500 below wholesale but with a black exterior, black interior and no air-conditioning, in the middle of a Florida summer.
To Albert J. Leo, general manager of Scott Buick in Pinellas Park, Fla., the Craven Bamboozle ads strike a raw nerve, but not so much because they are satirical, he said, but because they contain inaccuracies about leasing, a large part of his dealership's business.
"We feel the ads are degrading and demeaning and not 100 percent accurate," said Mr. Leo, who is president of the St. Petersburg Dealers Association.
Well, grit your teeth, Mr. Leo. Craven Bamboozle will ride again, as early as this Fourth of July weekend. the younger Mr. Swanson received a letter from Helen Jane Spicer of Clearwater, Fla., who praised Swanson Chrysler's ads and contributed a story about herself and a supposed industry practice known as "unhorsing."
A dealer convinces a customer to take home a car for a test drive overnight. The next day the customer is astonished to find that her old car has been "sold," without a title or a sales agreement. Mrs. Spicer said it happened to her.
"We just couldn't not do it; it's so Craven Bamboozle," Mr. Swanson said.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Dust Off the SEATO Charter

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
The foreign policy establishment is currently wrapped up in a mind-numbing debate about whether NATO should be enlarged. But if you think about it, the more urgent question for American foreign policy is whether SEATO should be revived.
Yes, you remember SEATO -- the "Southeast Asia Treaty Organization." Formed in 1954 by the Philippines, Thailand, Pakistan, Australia, New Zealand, Britain, France and the U.S., it aimed to deter China and the spread of Communism in Asia. SEATO was terminated in 1977, after the Vietnam war, leaving no trace outside of trivia quiz shows. 
 Of course, the old SEATO is not likely to rise from the ashes anytime soon, but think about this: The chances of the Russian military invading Eastern Europe today are virtually nil. Today's Russia has neither the intentions, nor, if Chechnya is any guide, the capabilities to threaten Eastern Europe. That is why we have the luxury of endlessly debating NATO enlargement. It can wait.
That is simply not the case anymore in Asia. There, a rapidly growing China increasingly has the capabilities, and some would argue the intentions, to expand aggressively into the South China Sea, to seize the oil-laden Spratly Islands and to more generally intimidate all its neighbors. Unlike Europe, where you at least have a core NATO alliance of 16 countries in place, Asia is a strategic no man's land. There is no formal security structure there, and the ad hoc balancing arrangements, involving the U.S., Japan, Korea and Russia, are just that -- ad hoc and uncertain, held together with Band-Aids and baling wire.
How long will this ad hoc structure be able to maintain a strategic balance with 1.2 billion Chinese, whose economy is growing at 10 percent per annum, whose work force adds 60 million job-seekers each year and whose huge army is steadily modernizing?
The optimists argue that the Chinese military, while improving, still has very little ability to project power far beyond its borders. It has no aircraft carriers, only a few old submarines and no aerial refueling capabilities. But when you have 3.2 million men under arms, you can be the Salvation Army and still intimidate your neighbors.
The pessimists argue that China has consistently lied about its defense budgets and strategic thinking. That makes its neighbors nervous, particularly when both the U.S. and Japan are slowing their defense spending and China is speeding its military modernization -- not to mention bullying Hong Kong, brazenly testing more nuclear weapons and beefing up its toeholds in the Spratly Islands, which are claimed by five other nations.
Senior U.S. officials say that this behavior has increasingly prompted some of China's neighbors to ask Washington whether the best policy in the coming decade is to engage China or formally try to contain it. The obsessive focus on NATO expansion has obscured this behind-the-scenes discussion.
At this stage, any formal containment of China would be premature. SEATO's resurrection can wait. Declaring Beijing the enemy now would only become a self-fulfilling prophecy. It may be inevitable, but as long as there is still a chance for shaping a benign China, the stakes are too big not to try.
The U.S. and its Asian allies need to stick with a combination of engagement and hidden containment. That means halting the shrinkage of the U.S. naval and army presence in Asia and establishing full diplomatic relations with Vietnam -- now -- because as China's historical enemy, Hanoi is one of the strongest counterweights to Beijing. It means getting Japan to understand that if it continues its unfair trade practices, it will corrode the strategic axis between Washington and Tokyo that is central to balancing China. And it also means conducting a sustained, high-level dialogue with China's leaders, and continuing to rope them into regional associations, like the APEC economic forum, in the hopes of taming their bullying instincts through interdependence.
It's the Woody Allen solution. His famous quip that "80 percent of success is showing up" is true in geopolitics as well. For now, formally trying to contain China would be a mistake. But we can keep showing up -- militarily, economically and diplomatically -- drawing red lines for China wherever necessary, building bridges wherever possible. And dusting off that SEATO charter just in case.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Scalping Revision Is Being Postponed

BYLINE: By RALPH BLUMENTHAL 

 BODY:
The curtain has all but come down for now on a disputed effort to scrap the state's scalping law and allow ticket brokers to set their own prices for resale of tickets to Broadway shows and special events.
With New York's limits on ticket markups set to expire on Friday, the Senate and Assembly have been considering bills that would allow licensed brokers to charge whatever they want for tickets. Out-of-state brokers have been lobbying strenuously in behalf of those bills, but it seems likely they will be shelved for a while. Unable to agree on the measures, the State Legislature is expected to extend the current, spottily enforced law through next March. 
 For theatergoers, it is likely to mean little change: tickets to popular shows will remain hard to get at the box office but available from brokers, particularly out of state, at high premiums.
Were the bills to pass, a broker who bought tickets from the box office at the listed price of, say, $60 a seat, could legally resell them for $150 or $200 a ticket or higher, depending on the demand, which is just what brokers in New Jersey and Connecticut now do in spite of the current law. New York brokers, however, are limited to a markup of $5 a ticket or 10 percent of the face price, whichever is higher, although in practice many add fees raising the markup to about 30 percent.
But now the proposed bills, which include a $2 per ticket surcharge to go to the state and which have been strongly opposed by theater owners and producers, have fallen short, giving way to compromise bills that will merely extend the current law through March 1996.
The action, or lack of it, is the latest milestone in the long effort to regulate the big business of selling access to live entertainment events, an issue that the previous New York State Attorney General, Oliver G. Koeppel, turned into something of an investigative crusade. His successor, Dennis Vacco, is pursuing the inquiries but has not taken a strong public stand. The investigation into what was called a booming black market in prime tickets continues to inch along with no sign of a conclusion. New Jersey recently voted to try a free market in ticket resales, while Connecticut voted to keep its anti-scalping limits in place for events in that state. And just this week, a standoff between the rock band Pearl Jam and Ticketmaster over the distributor's ticket markups culminated in cancellation of most of the band's summer tour.
The free-market bills were sponsored by Senator Dean Skelos, Republican of Nassau, and Assemblyman Joseph T. Pillittere. Democrat of Niagara County. A formal vote to extend the current law is expected sometime before the Legislature adjourns this week. "One thing we don't want to do," said Assemblyman Richard Brodsky, Democrat of Westchester, "is to back into a restructuring of the historic pricing system."
Even the East Coast Ticket Brokers Association, representing out-of-state brokers pressing for an open market, appeared to concede yesterday that the effort was dormant for now. The group's second choice, said Robert Malito, a legislative agent, or lobbyist, was to extend the present system and work toward a new compromise bill.
If all this seems like a lot of sound and fury over what should be the simple business of buying a seat to a show or concert, the reality is that it is hardly a simple business. That was evident at an Assembly hearing last month, at which one main issue was whether lifting the constraints on ticket resale would actually lower ticket prices or send them through the roof.
It all boiled down, several speakers said, to an issue of fairness. Ticket buyers need to feel that they have a fair shot at getting good seats and not that the best seats are being squirreled away for profiteering. Also, several legislators said, Broadway is too treasured an asset to jeopardize by pricing experiments.
Barry Lefkowitz, executive director of the year-old brokers association, argued that removing the restrictions would "bring the industry out of the shadows and the underground," and help "end the black market" and lure brokers from Connecticut and New Jersey back to New York City, lowering prices.
But Assemblyman Brodsky took issue with his economics. He asked what sense there was to taking the limits off resale prices when there was no flexibility in the supply. He said, "All that's going to do is shoot the price through the ceiling."
Mr. Lefkowitz contended in turn that in four states where a free market was recently instituted, Texas, California, Illinois and Indiana, resale ticket prices have in fact dropped. He also argued that eliminating New York's markup limits would restore the competitive advantage of New York's brokers, who now cannot get the best tickets to offer clients.
Reflecting the opposition of many theater owners and producers to a free market in ticket resales, Gerald Schoenfeld, chairman of the Shubert Organization, said the brokers "should not be trafficking in our product." Allowing unlimited markups, he said, would only increase the pressure on box office employees to conspire to funnel tickets to brokers, which is already a big problem. In a free market, Mr. Schoenfeld added, theater owners and producers might themselves raise ticket prices to "legitimately share in the true value of our ticket."
In the end, one mystery remained: the sources of the tickets regularly sold at high markups by the out-of-state brokers and the scalpers who haunt theatrical and other entertainment events. "I've heard of these things, but I've never seen such a situation," said Mr. Lefkowitz of the brokers' group.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tickets to some of Broadway's shows. (Dith Pran/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Voters Spurn a Tax Rise In Orange County Crisis

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: SANTA ANA, Calif., June 27

 BODY:
Orange County voters today soundly rejected a half-cent increase in the sales tax to help bail their government out of bankruptcy, increasing worries about how the county would pay its debts and avoid drastic cuts in its schools and municipal services.
With 60 percent of the vote running against the measure after a third of the precincts were counted, backers conceded that they had failed. 
 In the past six weeks of campaigning, politics has taken precedence over policy as the county wrestles with the consequences of its $1.7 billion loss last December in bad investments made by county officials and their financial advisers in the bond market. As a deadline for repayment of $975 million in bond debt approaches this summer, neither the opponents nor proponents had prepared a clear recovery plan in the event that the tax increase was defeated.
The immediate outcome of the rejection was uncertainty. Without the $130 million that the tax would reap each year over its 10-year life, officials feared that the county would not have the money to repay its huge debts, forcing significant cutbacks in government and social services and even raising the prospect of a state takeover.
In a possible harbinger of future problems with the bond market, the county today found itself facing rejection when it tried to sell $155 million in new bonds. It had to offer unusually high interest rates on the bonds and it still had difficulty attracting investors. "There was a general buyers' strike," said Paul Nussbaum, a county financial adviser. "It was a clear-cut boycott on Orange County paper. Price was not an issue."
In a statement today, State Treasurer Matt Fong warned that the state could become more directly involved in county operations. "This remains a county problem and time is running out," he said. "It may escalate to a statewide problem if there is either a default or a repudiation of a legal debt."
But that is not a step most people in state government relish. Gov. Pete Wilson vetoed a bill that would have given him the tools to take over most county operations. And political analysts said the state was reluctant either to become directly involved in the crisis or to use taxpayer funds to rescue one of the most affluent counties in the nation.
Orange County still hopes it will succeed in a difficult deal to defer, or roll over, its bond debt for a year, thus avoiding a default and buying time for more cuts and possibly a new effort to increase the sales tax.
"I think there is a high probability that a rollover will be successfully negotiated," said James L. Doti, an economic analyst who is president of Chapman University in the city of Orange and who backed the tax referendum, Measure R. In this rich, conservative county that many people regard as ground zero in the nation's anti-tax movement, the notion of raising the sales tax a half percent to 8.25 percent to repair the the damage caused by government officials has aroused widespread anger. On the other hand, the alternatives that have been put forward are surrounded by ifs.
Larry Kimbell, who heads an economic forecasting group at the University of California at Los Angeles, said, "There are going to have to be some bloody cuts, some big, bloody cuts, and even then there will still probably be a financial crisis."
Opponents of the tax have placed their hopes in a number of steps that economic analysts say are far less certain to solve the problems than a sales tax increase.
They have called for cuts in the government bureaucracy, and propose the sale of John Wayne Airport and the diversion of highway funds that many analysts say may be legally impossible. And they hope for a huge settlement from Merrill Lynch, which is being sued by the county for its role in the failed investments.
Pro-tax campaigners acknowledged misgivings that residents of this seaside county had not yet felt enough pain from the bankruptcy to impose a tax on themselves.
And if the voters of Orange County are not willing to feel some pain through a higher sales tax, Mr. Kimbell said, it is unlikely that state legislators in Sacramento or the county' creditors in the bond market will be willing to bail them out.
At the state level, he added, "If Orange County refuses to raise taxes, what are the odds that Californians would be happy to vote more taxes to bail them out? Zero."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Japan's Trade Minister Is Sounding Flexible
 Things looked bad in Geneva until Japan's trade minister perked up at some "creative alternatives" offered by Mickey Kantor and surprised the Americans with a new willingness to expand foreign access to the Japanese auto market. As tonight's deadline approached, though, it was not clear whether Japan would offer enough to persuade President Clinton to call off threatened tariffs on luxury cars. [Page A1.] 
 The myth: Detroit has banged persistently on Japan's door, only to be left with wilted sedans. The truth: the Big Three auto makers have barely tapped. [D4.]
 Europe has squeezed concessions out of Japan with a tough trade stance -- the kind of concessions Tokyo denies Washington. [D4.]
 
No Orange County Tax Increase
 Voters soundly rejected a sales tax increase intended to help Orange County, Calif., recover from its bankruptcy. The rejection added to Wall Street's worries about the county's ability to pay its debts. [A10.]
 
Young Smokers: Philip Morris Acts
 Philip Morris announced a broad campaign to keep minors from smoking, including nationwide ads, warning labels and the end of promotional giveaways. But critics shot back that the company is trying to undercut Federal efforts to regulate tobacco as a drug and state efforts to penalize sales to minors. [A14.]
 The Government will keep subsidizing tobacco growers, as it has for 50 years. [A14.]
 
A Selloff in H.M.O. Stocks
 Nearly every H.M.O. stock came under attack, with the selloff even including shares of United Healthcare -- despite the hopes surrounding a planned merger. Market Place. [D8.]
 Stocks fell in volatile trading, with the Dow off 8.64, to 4,542.61, after being up 20 earlier. Prime suspects: conflicting reports on the economy and interest-rate worries. [D8.]
 
Blow to New York Water Plan
 Calling it a risky "fiscal gimmick," Comptroller Alan G. Hevesi vowed to block a plan to sell New York City's water system to a quasi-independent public agency. [A1.]
 
Too Many Presidential Economists?
 Do enough economists hover at the Presidential elbow? Some members of a House subcommittee say so, and are trying to kill the Council of Economic Advisers, but the effort has been criticized by past council chairmen from both parties. [D2.]
 
The S.E.C. Wasn't Happy
 It was billed as a "Snow White" sequel, with vast revenue. But the S.E.C. said investors in "Happily Ever After" were defrauded. [D2.]
 
Pollution: Speak No Evil?
 Manufacturers are required to disclose how much they pollute, imposing little burden beyond the paperwork. Yet the Senate's regulation-slayers are trying to halt the rapid rise of such disclosure. [A16.]
 
Mexico Helps Newly Private Banks
 Mexico is pouring billions into its weaker banks to keep them afloat in the financial crisis. Bolder steps may be ahead. [D5.]
 
A Car Dealer Skewers His Own
 It sounds crazy: a car dealer runs ads starring a cartoon character called Craven Bamboozle, who would sell his own mother a lemon. But for a Florida dealer, it seems to be working. Advertising. [D7.]
 
Easing Travel to China
 The opening of an American Express business travel center in Beijing should ease Chinese travel. Business Travel. [D6.]

HEADLINE: U.S. OFFICIALS SAY JAPAN EASES A BIT ON TRADE STANCE

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: GENEVA, Wednesday, June 28

 BODY:
As American sanctions loomed against Japanese luxury cars, Japan's Trade Minister showed new willingness to expand foreign access to the Japanese automotive market, American officials said early this morning. But it was not clear whether Tokyo would offer enough to persuade President Clinton to call off the 100 percent tariffs that are set to take effect within 24 hours.
The sudden flexibility by the Japanese Minister of International Trade and Industry, Ryutaro Hashimoto, clearly took American officials by surprise. Mr. Hashimoto and Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, met three times on Tuesday, ending their final session at 1:45 this morning. A Japanese official said the negotiators planned to meet again late this morning. 
 When the final session broke up this morning, it was not clear if the talks had reached the final stage of dealing with the most contentious issues, including commitments by Japan's auto makers to buy more American-made components.
"The impression we have is that he wants a deal," a senior American official said of Mr. Hashimoto. "But it is still unclear how far he can go."
If an agreement comes together, it would hinge on a side document, agreed to by Japan's car makers but not signed by the Japanese Government, that would lay out a series of "voluntary plans" for increased purchases of American auto parts.
Japanese officials said that the United States had proposed to word any such separate agreement in a way that assures the Japanese car industry that it would not be subject to penalties even if the car manufacturers failed to meet a series of benchmarks set out in the agreement. Those benchmarks would aim to verify that Japan's auto industry was buying an ever-increasing number of American components.
Officials were closeted in the meeting and unavailable to comment in detail on the latest reports.
Any such deal -- which still appeared to be just out of reach this morning -- could carry political risks on both sides of the Pacific, with each country undoubtedly tempted to describe the accord in different terms. Washington could be counted on to say that it won a major victory by getting Japan to agree to "measurable progress" in increasing parts purchases. Tokyo would certainly insist that it had defeated American efforts to get Japan to agree to "numerical targets."
The Japanese Government, by not being a party to that part of the accord, could argue that it had not engaged in "managed trade," even if it had choreographed every move.
The feverish late-hours maneuvering was a marked turnaround from the negotiating earlier on Tuesday. In the morning, top Administration officials in Washington had joined in a conference call with Mr. Kantor at which he described, in very pessimistic terms, his first negotiating session with Mr. Hashimoto, one of Japan's top politicians and a leading candidate to be the next prime minister. "It sounded like this whole thing was over, that there was no progress," said one official familiar with the briefing.
But a few hours later, Mr. Hashimoto's tone changed, and he discussed in great detail a paper handed to him by Mr. Kantor that laid out what one American official called "creative alternatives."
With the talks at a critical stage, Mr. Clinton faces a difficult choice today: take what Tokyo is offering now or move ahead with the sanctions on the bet that another, perhaps better, deal lies ahead once the Japanese car makers begin to feel some financial pain. American negotiators have made it clear that they believe the Japanese car makers have a backup plan, one they may not be willing to disclose until after sanctions go into effect at 12:01 A.M. Eastern time Thursday in the United States.
Mr. Clinton has little to lose politically in most areas of the country from placing the 100 percent tariffs on the wholesale value of 13 luxury models shipped to the United States. After two years of frustrating negotiations, he has been pressed to take a hard line by several members of his Administration.
In Portland, Ore., on Tuesday at a daylong discussion of the economy of the Pacific Rim, Mr. Clinton defended his Administration's stance, speaking in a region of the country heavily dependent on trade with Asia. He said his approach was not intended to be protectionist, but was simply necessary to pry open markets for American products.
"I want to tell you, to the people of the Pacific Northwest, I am not trying to launch a new era of protectionism," Mr. Clinton said. "But we have tried now for two or three decades to open this market and this is the last major block to developing a sensible global economic policy."
But others in the Administration are less comfortable with the strategy of pressing Japan to the wall, especially because of criticism that the United States is undercutting the World Trade Organization, the dispute-settlement body that Mr. Clinton strongly supported when he pressed Congress last fall to adopt the global trade pact.
One reason it is so difficult to predict an outcome is that the American negotiators say they are still uncertain who is making many of the decisions for the Japanese.
Mr. Hashimoto is clearly in the lead and, like Mr. Kantor, he has demonstrated that he prefers to cut a deal after engaging in a lot of back-and-forth that allows him to take the measure of his opponent.
For all his dominance of the Japanese side, however, Mr. Hashimoto cannot act alone. While the bureaucrats who have spent their lives working at the Ministry of International Trade and Industry have been divided over the merits of a deal, the lead negotiator, Vice Minister Yoshihiro Sakamoto, is widely viewed as reluctant to make concessions. He has repeatedly said that Japan has a solid case against the sanctions at the World Trade Organization and should press its argument there.
The unknown factor is the political calculation in Japan of the effect of a prolonged dispute with the United States. Tokyo officials worry that the dollar could plunge further, making it harder for all Japanese manufacturers to sell their products at a profit in the American market. Also, political pressure could build in Japan for retaliatory measures.
Mr. Hashimoto, at least in public, is keeping expectations low. Late last night, in one of his few comments to reporters, he said, "I was never optimistic from the beginning." When asked whether an agreement was at hand, he simply said: "I don't know."
In another overture to the United States by a Japanese auto maker, the Toyota Motor Corporation was expected to announce as early as today in Tokyo its plan to increase production of cars in the United States and other countries. The plan is also likely to include promises to increase research and development outside Japan and perhaps to increase local procurements of parts for foreign factories.
Japan's other major auto makers are also preparing such plans but might not announce them unless required to do so to bring a successful conclusion to the negotiations.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Japan Gets Economic Tonic; Critics Call It Bland

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 27

 BODY:
The Government announced a package of measures today meant to ward off recession in Japan, but economists dismissed the steps as unlikely to make much difference and investors responded by selling off shares.
The reaction underscored fears that if the Government was unable to stimulate the economy, conditions might worsen before they improve. As Japan moves into its fifth year of economic troubles -- and its eighth rescue package -- the fragile economy is teetering on the brink of recession. Last week, the Government reported that the economy had expanded a mere one-tenth of 1 percent in the first quarter of 1995 from the prior three months. 
 A stronger Japanese economy would help the United States as well, so officials in Washington have been prodding the Tokyo Government for stimulative measures in the hope they would entice Japanese consumers to buy more American products.
In its latest package, the Government offered a relatively broad array of measures and indicated it would back up its revitalization program with additional funds later in the year. But today's package nonetheless seemed to reflect the Government's reluctance to take any bold steps before elections are held next month.
With a political battle looming, and the possibility of shifts in the national leadership, the nation's economic woes have been a sensitive issue. In describing the plan announced today, Government officials seemed hesitant even to hint at the severity of the economy's troubles. As late as this morning, the text of the announcement was modified to remove language referring to the growing "concern over the future" of Japan's economy.
Instead, the Government tried to reassure the public, with Kozo Igarashi, a senior Cabinet official, saying, "We are convinced that the measures certainly will bring about an economic recovery."
Jesper J. Koll, an economist at J. P. Morgan Securities Asia Ltd., said, "Today's announcement highlights the lack of leadership from either the bureaucracy or the politicians."
But Mr. Koll cautioned that the Government's role might be limited even if it were to offer a plan seen as more effective. "The perception, unfortunately, is that there is someone in the Government who can push a button and the economy can go back to 3 percent," he said. "We're getting patchwork packages that plaster over some of the cracks in the system. But it's difficult to gut the entire building and construct something entirely new."
Still, some economists said today's measures, however meager, were nudging the rescue attempt in the right direction. "They felt a need to put something out that shows they have a broad-based conception of what needs to be done," said Robert Alan Feldman, an economist at Salomon Brothers Asia Ltd. "The energy level is higher."
Investors, however, snubbed the measures. Stock prices, already slipping in the morning, fell further after details of the package were released in the early afternoon. The benchmark Nikkei 225 stock index tumbled 386.31 points, or 2.6 percent, to close at 14,759.05.
[The decline of the Nikkei was continuing on Wednesday. At midday, the index was down another 126.14 points, or almost nine-tenths of 1 percent, at 14,632.91.]
The Government package did not provide fresh money to stimulate the economy. But it would extend existing tax cuts and take some funds allocated for public works projects in the second half of the fiscal year, which began on April 1, and spend them in the first six months.
The Government would spend 14.5 trillion yen, or $173 million, before September. That amount, which is more than 70 percent of the spending planned for public works in the full year, represents 3 percent of Japan's economic output.
In what might be a controversial step, the Government suggested that it would consider expanding the use of public funds to help support the nation's ailing banks, which have a bad reputation among Japanese consumers. A council is being formed to study how such funds could be used, but there were few details, and some economists said the measures offered little more than the Government's earlier pledge to stand behind the banking system.
Perhaps as a bone to the United States, which has been pushing for Japan to further open its markets, the package included a general plan to evaluate ways to increase foreign investment and improve access to the Japanese market. But rather than offering numerical targets for buying imports or specific measures to open markets, the Government said instead that it would publish a white paper next month on deregulating the economy.
The package did suggest that as part of its effort to deregulate the economy, the Government might adopt quantitative goals for bringing prices in Japan more in line with prices elsewhere in the world. American imports are often twice as expensive in Japan as at home.
International investors, who have had a big role in the Tokyo and Osaka stock exchanges, have been intensely concerned about Japan's ailing stock market and financial system. Today's measures included provisions to create a second over-the-counter market and suspend the dividend tax on stock repurchases. But with the stock and real estate markets depressed, the steps offered only modest hope for a revival.

A chart in Business Day on Wednesday with an article about Japanese economic policy misstated the trend in Japan's economy in the fourth quarter of 1994. Japan's gross domestic product contracted 1 percent in that quarter (at an annual rate); it did not grow.

HEADLINE: A DEAL ON AUTO TRADE;
U.S. Dealers in Japanese Cars Relieved at Escaping Big Tariffs

 BODY:
Yesterday's trade agreement averts the punitive tariffs that would have made Japanese luxury cars so expensive in America that practically no one would have bought them. Auto importers hailed the accord, saying it would preserve jobs at dealerships that sell the Lexus, Infiniti, Acura and other models.
Typical was the statement from Nissan North America, which handles Infiniti imports. "The real winners in this settlement," said Timothy MacCarthy, vice president of government affairs for the importer, "are the thousands of dealers and employees, whose jobs were saved, and American consumers, who will continue to benefit from free trade and competition." 
 The statement added: "We want to assure our customers that Infiniti dealers have a good supply of vehicles in inventory. Nissan plans to resume its normal production schedule immediately."
Toyota's American arm also praised the agreement, issuing a statement that said, "We at Toyota, our Lexus dealers and their employees are relieved that good sense prevailed on both sides of the table."

HEADLINE: Human Rights Group Denied Accreditation for U.N. Event

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, June 28

 BODY:
Freedom House, the New York-based human rights organization that monitors political liberties, civil rights and press freedom worldwide, has been denied participation in United Nations events as an accredited unofficial group.
Several other organizations faced challenges last week from a loose coalition of nations, including China, Cuba, India, Indonesia, Iran and the Sudan, which has been campaigning to curtail the growing influence of human rights groups in international affairs. These countries are all members of a committee within the Economic and Social Council that recommends what organizations should be given the right to take part in United Nations conferences and other meetings. 
 When the committee ended its meetings last week, only Freedom House among dozens of applicants had been rebuffed. More than 1,000 other unofficial groups are already accredited.
Another application that was challenged was submitted by Physicians for Human Rights, a Boston-based group that does extensive volunteer work for the United Nations at its own expense, including exhuming victims of genocide, testing DNA samples to reunite families after civil wars and advising war crimes panels. But the group's application was finally approved when India, which objected to the organization's reporting on the medical evidence of military atrocities in Kashmir, apparently was unable to gain enough support to block it, diplomats said.
The Economic and Social Council, meeting in Geneva in July, has the final say on what groups are finally admitted. But Freedom House does not expect to succeed in overriding the decision made on June 19.
"Ecosoc is not under any compulsion to accept," said Christopher Kean, projects director for Freedom House, using the diplomatic shorthand for the Economic and Social Council. "But people I've spoken to cannot remember a time when Ecosoc has gone against a recommendation by the committee."
James P. Rubin, spokesman for Madeleine K. Albright, the American representative at the United Nations, said today that the United States "strongly supports Freedom House's application."
"It is particularly disturbing that an organization like Freedom House, which is effective in shedding light on abuses should be opposed in this way, and we are going to continue to work on this issue," he said. The United States delegation has also been battling obstacles to wide participation in an international conference on women in Beijing in August and September.
In the 1950's at the height of the cold war, Freedom House had been accused of being the recipient of Central Intelligence Agency money. This accusation was never substantiated. In an interview tonight, Leonard Sussman, the director of the organization for 21 years and now its senior international scholar, dismissed the assertion, and said that from its inception in 1941 the organization had supported both conservative and liberal causes.
Nine countries voted to admit Freedom House: Britain, Bulgaria, Chile, Costa Rica, Greece, Ireland, Paraguay, Russia and the United States. Nine voted against: China, Cuba, Ethiopia, India, Indonesia, Philippines, the Sudan, Swaziland and Tunisia. Madagascar was absent. In case of a tie, an application is rejected.
"The Paraguayans and Russians put the case best," Mr. Kean said in an interview today. "They said that they did not agree with what we had said about them, but that democracy is about accepting differences of opinion." The Philippines apparently decided to vote against Freedom House after being shown how Manila rated in the organization's last report, in which the country was described as "partly free."
At the Committee to Protect Journalists, Bill Orme, the executive director, said that his group had decided not to fight a long battle for so-called consultative status in the United Nations, but were willing to apply for similar access to Unesco.
Unesco had encouraged their application, Mr. Orme said. But then they were turned down on the ground that the Committee to Protect Journalists was not an international organization.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

U.S. and Japan Set Deal With Just Hours to Go
With just hours left before $5.9 billion in sanctions were to kick in, the United States and Japan found common ground on automotive trade. After long, turbulent talks, the key bargainers proclaimed a new era for two scrapping nations. But a close look shows an accord that is often deliberately vague and may fall short of President Clinton's goal of cracking open Japan's markets. [Page A1.]
 
On Capitol Hill, praise was cautious and muted. Some Democrats wanted more, while Republicans fretted about confrontational tactics. And Phil Gramm said, "The President was only shooting blanks." [D7.] 

Japanese auto companies will now -- surprise! -- do exactly what they were planning to do: become even tougher competitors here. [D6.]
 
"We didn't cave in," said a Japanese trade official, insisting that Tokyo had agreed to no targets, had specified no figures. [D6.]
 
China hungers for cars, Detroit is eager, but bureaucracy and logistics still intrude. [D7.]
 
Senate Passes Lawsuit Limits
A measure to discourage shareholder lawsuits passed the Senate by a surprisingly large margin after a flood of ads on both sides. Backing it were accounting firms and brokers, while consumer groups and regulators opposed it, concerned that duped investors would have little recourse. [A1.]
 
New Head for Endangered Council
The Council of Economic Advisers got a new chairman -- even as House Republicans moved closer to ending its financing. [A18.]
 
"It would be a pity to throw away a bargain" -- that summed up the reaction of many economists to the notion of scrapping the council. Peter Passell: Economic Scene. [D2.]
 
Monsanto to Acquire Calgene Stake
Monsanto will buy half of Calgene, known for its genetically engineered tomato. [D3.]
 
Healthy Profits at Morgan Stanley
Morgan Stanley reported a healthy increase in quarterly profits. [D5.]
 
Disclosure on Pension Plans
If their pension plans have too little money to pay promised benefits, big companies must break that news to workers this year -- and warn them of possible consequences. [D3.]
 
T.W.A. to Try Bankruptcy, Again
T.W.A. is trying bankruptcy again, but this time it hopes to emerge with less debt and a more realistic strategy. [D3.]
 
Prudential Is Sued Over New Era
Prudential Securities has been sued by two charities and a philanthropist seeking to recoup some money they stand to lose in the New Era bankruptcy. [D5.]
 
USAir? What Does Buffett Know?
USAir looked just fine to two big institutional investors even though the great Warren Buffett gave it a thumbs down. Sure enough, the stock doubled. Market Place. [D10.]
 
Stocks Rally, Then Fade
Stocks rallied strongly on the trade agreement, then slipped on weakness of technology shares. Still, the Dow industrials ended up 14.18 points. [D10.]
 
Broadway, in a Plain Wrapper
Beef. Milk. Broadway. National commodities all -- at least that's the goal of theater marketers who want people "to think of Broadway as a brand" and whose ads read "Celebrate Broadway" and "Destination Broadway." Advertising. [D8.]
 
"HIStory": No Sales Thriller
Michael Jackson's "HIStory" sold 391,000 copies its first week, making it No. 1 on the pop charts. Sony expected more. [C14.]
