HEADLINE: Chicago's Mayor Gains School Control That New York's Mayor Would Envy

BYLINE: By DON TERRY 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, June 28

 BODY:
Mayor Richard M. Daley will essentially take over the city's schools on Thursday with the announcement of his handpicked choices for a "superboard," a scaled-down panel of five members, to run the nation's third-largest school system.
The move is the culmination of what could prove to be a decisive springtime for public education in Chicago, whose school system has for years grappled with financial crises, low test scores and high dropout rates. 
 In one of the last acts of the lame-duck 15-member school board, eight schools, including highly regarded Lindblom High, were recently shut down because they had been underutilized. And in what some education monitors here regard as a fitting farewell to that board, its president, D. Sharon Grant, resigned on Tuesday, four days before the end of her term, indicted on charges of having failed to file Federal income tax returns since 1977.
The centerpiece of the effort to turn the schools around was a bill, enacted by the State Legislature this spring, that gave Mr. Daley the kind of power that other big-city mayors, certainly Rudolph W. Giuliani of New York, would wish for themselves: the right to select the head of the city schools -- and to do so without the advice or consent of any legislative or educational body.
Mr. Daley will also appoint every other top decision-maker for the system, which has 410,000 students, 45,000 employees and a budget of $2.9 billion. And his former chief of staff, Gery Chico, is expected to be the first president of the new board, which takes control at the end of this week.
The change in Chicago is much like that sought by Mr. Giuliani in New York. "We were astonished to find it was almost a mirror image of what we've been advocating in New York City," said Richard J. Schwartz, senior adviser to Mr. Giuliani. But legislators in Albany have proved far less eager than those in Illinois to give the Mayor such sweeping control over education.
Indeed, even supporters of the change here temper their optimism with some expressions of uneasiness.
"It concerns me that we've given that much power to a single political actor," said John Ayers, executive director of Leadership for Quality Education, a business group that backed the legislation. "I think in my gut it's going to work out, but it's going to be a bit of a white-knuckle ride."
Linda Lenz, editor and publisher of The Catalyst, a magazine that monitors school reform efforts in Chicago, called the overhaul "stunning."
"It's an opportunity for great good," she said, "or great catastrophe."
Under the newly enacted law, the Mayor's complete control will expire after four years. At that point, the board will grow to seven members, all of whom will require City Council confirmation. In the meantime, said Fred Hess, executive director of the Chicago Panel on School Policy, a group whose particular interest is school finances, Mr. Daley has the power of an autocrat.
"My guess is it will work for getting the management of the schools back in line," Mr. Hess said. "But the bad part is he has complete control of jobs, contracts and patronage."
Already the Chicago Teachers Union has gone to court in an effort to block enforcement of a part of the law that bars teachers from striking for the next 18 months and limits the scope of their collective bargaining to salaries and benefits. Class size, long a subject of contract negotiations, will now be set by the new school board.
But Jackie Gallagher, a spokeswoman for the 32,000-member teachers union, said it had no objection to the Mayor's appointing a new board. She called the boards of the last 15 years "dismal failures."
"I don't know what's going to happen with the Mayor," she said, "but can he do any worse?"
The Mayor's authority to appoint members to the school board dates from long before the new law. But the old authority was extremely limited, because he had to choose from a list of candidates submitted to him by a nominating commission that often included his political enemies.
Now the Democratic Mayor has all the authority he could have wanted, delivered to him by a Republican-controlled Legislature that has effectively shut off his ability to place blame for the poor state of the schools on his powerlessness to do anything about it.
"This is Republican legislation that they handed him," said the Mayor's spokesman, Jim Williams. "But he's not running away from it. He's often said, 'If you hold me responsible, give me the authority to make sweeping and profound change.' Now he has that authority."
Only time will tell whether the Legislature has handed Mr. Daley a noose instead of a gift. That Legislature did not include in its package what many experts say is more crucial to the ultimate success of the schools here: more money to run a system that in recent years has had to do everything but collect cans in the alley to make ends meet. "Without more dollars," Mr. Ayers said, "it's kind of like a gift that eats."
The school system must patch a $150 million budget deficit for the coming school year, and the following year looks even worse. No one expects much help from the state, which ranks near the bottom nationally in state aid to school districts, and the Mayor has said that finding additional city money will be extremely difficult.
The new changes leave in place the basic elements of the city's last round of school reform, in 1988. That year parents, neighborhood advocates and school staff were given much more say in the day-to-day running of the schools, from selecting principals to picking out what books students read.
As a result, school reform groups say, there has been some improvement. Test scores are inching up, and the dropout rate has dipped to 42 percent in 1994 from 51 percent in 1991. Overcrowding persists, however, particularly in Hispanic neighborhoods, while other neighborhoods have lost schools because there were not enough students.
What Anne Hallett and Diana Lauber of the Cross City Campaign for Urban School Reform want to seenow is even more local control.
A committed reform movement, Ms. Lauber said, has been built in the last few years, and it will be closely watching Mr. Daley's every step.

HEADLINE: A DEAL ON AUTO TRADE: THE AGREEMENT;
U.S. SETTLES TRADE DISPUTE, AVERTING BILLIONS IN TARIFFS ON JAPANESE LUXURY AUTOS

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: GENEVA, June 28

 BODY:
The United States and Japan reached a broad but ambiguously worded accord today on automotive trade, ending a two-year dispute just hours before $5.9 billion in tariffs against Japanese luxury cars were scheduled to go into effect.
President Clinton canceled the trade sanctions after a settlement was reached in marathon and turbulent negotiations here between the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, and his Japanese counterpart, Ryutaro Hashimoto. 
 The talks over expanding American access to the Japanese automotive market were so heated that as late as 8 A.M. here, the effort to strike a deal had all but collapsed. It was pieced together anew, Japanese and American officials said, only after Mr. Kantor asked Mr. Hashimoto to come to the small United States trade office. Mr. Kantor then told Mr. Hashimoto, "Ryu, if we don't do it now, it won't get done."
The two negotiators emerged from that office 10 hours later, clearly exhausted, heralding the accord as the beginning of a new era for two countries whose economic disputes had begun to tear at their political alliance. "This agreement is a significant step to fundamental change," Mr. Kantor said. "It is broad, detailed and quantifiable."
In Washington, the President, who had vowed to go ahead with the sanctions unless Japan relented, said the agreement "will not solve every problem in our relationship, but for today we have proof that hard bargaining and good faith can overcome apparently insurmountable conflict."
It was far from clear, however, whether Mr. Clinton had met his own oft-stated goal of reaching a landmark accord that would break the back of Japan's tightly linked network of manufacturers and suppliers, forcing them to change forever their buying habits.
While the accord seems likely to increase purchases from the United States, the specifics described by both sides pointed to an accord that was deliberately vague in many crucial sections. No text of the actual agreement was released today. Nor does the agreement constitute a binding commitment by either the Japanese Government or Japan's car makers to increase substantially the purchase of American parts.
The breakthrough was greeted with relief in financial markets. The dollar rose against all major currencies as investors concluded that the deal would help avert a trade war. Stocks and bonds also rallied.
In the negotiations, the Japanese Government agreed to make major changes in its regulatory system that should make it easier for American companies to sell replacement parts in Japanese repair shops, which until now have been strictly controlled by the car makers. A far broader number of garages will now be permitted to conduct repairs for the strict automobile inspection that the Japanese call the "shaken." An inspection often leads to several thousand dollars worth of repairs. In opening that market to competition, the United States is relying on expectations that more shops will use American-made parts.
Tokyo also endorsed a substantial increase in the number of dealers who handle American cars, a figure that Mr. Kantor put at 200 more dealerships within a year, and 1,000 by the end of the decade. But again Mr. Hashimoto said in writing that "the Government of Japan has had no involvement in this forecast because it is beyond the scope and responsibility of government."
In Tokyo, the agreement was generally seen as not requiring Japan's major auto makers to do much more than they would have done anyway.
Japan's five major automobile companies released "voluntary plans" today that Mr. Clinton estimated would amount to approximately $9 billion in added sales for American companies over the next three years.
But many of the company-issued plans had few, if any, hard numbers. The Japanese Government stipulated in writing that any purchase estimates were not formal commitments, did not carry Government backing and were not enforceable. Nor did Japan endorse Mr. Kantor's arithmetic. An announcement issued by the two officials said: "Minister Hashimoto said that Ambassador Kantor's estimates are his own, and neither shared by the Minister himself nor the Government of Japan."
Mr. Kantor released his own breakdown of those estimates, indicating that more than two-thirds of the increased purchases, about $6.75 billion, would be met by Japanese auto plants situated in the United States. That would be an addition to current purchases of about $17 billion. The suppliers would include American car parts companies and Japanese companies that have operations in the United States, and are regarded as American companies for the purposes of the agreement.
In a sign of the chaos that surrounded the last few hours of the negotiations, that statement was included on a one-page document, apparently used in the negotiations, that was mistakenly released by Mr. Kantor's office. The document still had blanks for estimated parts purchases, suggesting that the final figures were only settled upon at the last minute.
On one critical issue the American negotiators clearly got far less than they wanted. Japanese auto makers did agree to use more foreign-made components in automobile factories in Japan, but much less than originally sought by the Administration. The issue was considered critical to breaking the "keiretsu" system of suppliers, which has been blamed for many of the troubles American companies encounter in Japan.
The Japanese companies agreed to import $6 billion in foreign parts for their domestic operations by 1997, a figure that combined purchases from the United States, Europe and Asia. Previously, Japanese companies set the same goal for 1994, but did not get even halfway to it, importing about $2.5 billion in parts. Meanwhile, Europeans have insisted that every benefit offered to American companies must also apply to them, a requirement that was endorsed in today's deal.
"We did the best we could on this," Mr. Kantor said. "Given the state of the Japanese economy, we had to be realistic."
While the specifics appear vague, "we have set a standard," Mr. Kantor said. "This is more than moral suasion, this is two Governments saying what we expect the companies to do. It is not something the firms can ignore."
Mr. Kantor also estimated that the five major Japanese auto companies would increase their production of cars in the United States to 2.65 million units by 1998, up from 2.1 million now. But it is unclear how much of that would have happened without the political pressure from the Administration.
Mr. Hashimoto agreed to send a letter to Japanese auto dealers telling them that they were free to carry foreign vehicles, but it was questionable how much difference such a letter would make.
Arguments over the numbers -- what they would amount to, and whether they would be published at all, even as estimates -- occupied much of the last-minute wrangling.
The deal was apparently nearly in place on Tuesday night, when Mr. Kantor finished his talks with Mr. Hashimoto at about 1:45 A.M., and the deputies working for the two men continued to talk through the night. Mr. Kantor soon regretted leaving the talks, even for a few hours.
The bureaucrats who run the Ministry of International Trade and Industry began unwrapping the deal, American officials said, pulling back from some of Mr. Hashimoto's concessions. "The whole thing blew up," a senior American official said. So at 8 A.M. Mr. Kantor asked Mr. Hashimoto over to the American trade office and issued something of an ultimatum. Time was running short, he said, and if the ministry bureaucrats were not brought under control, the sanctions would go into effect.
It worked. At midday, one of the top negotiators reported that the Japanese "have moved an awful lot in the last three hours."
By then, Mr. Kantor was left with the clear choice of accepting an agreement that fell short of what he originally wanted -- particularly on the purchase of American parts for factories in Japan -- or risking the political and diplomatic fallout of allowing the sanctions to be put on Japanese luxury cars. He decided, officials said, that he was unlikely to extract substantially more from Japan after the sanctions went into effect. Whether that was an accurate judgment will probably be debated for months to come.
One of the biggest fears was that sanctions could set off another plunge in the dollar. Another general worry was over what would happen to the American-Japanese relationship, which has been on uneasy ground for years.
"We were ready to go to sanctions, without question," a senior Administration official said. "But it is a big step, something you don't do to an ally lightly. So if there was a way to avoid it, we wanted to. And it was very speculative about whether, if we went to sanctions, we would get more."

HEADLINE: Diplomas Are Denied to 17 In Washington Irving Furor

BYLINE: By SARAH KERSHAW 

 BODY:
At 8:30 A.M. yesterday, the new graduates filed into the main office at Washington Irving High School and collected their diplomas. But 17 of their classmates, whose diplomas had seemed certain at the graduation ceremony on Monday, were not among them.
Their principal, it was found, had improperly intervened to graduate them even though they had failed required courses. Some teachers had complained that the principal, Robert B. Durkin, had bypassed their authority, and by early Tuesday evening, Schools Chancellor Ramon C. Cortines had removed Mr. Durkin from his job, effective Friday. He also ordered a review of the academic records of 19 students. 
 The review found that two of the students had done enough work to qualify them for graduation. In one case, the principal intervened with a hefty economics assignment; in another he allowed a student who had missed too many gym classes to make up the credits through an after-school dance program. But the fate of the other 17 students was uncertain; their diplomas were still being withheld yesterday, according to Board of Education officials.
On a day that would normally be spent with students signing yearbooks, making summer plans, exchanging addresses and relishing the end of high school, the diplomas the Washington Irving seniors waited years to touch, wave and show off became a symbol of scandal at the school on Irving Place in Manhattan.
"Every time we go somewhere to get a job, they're going to think the diploma is not legitimate -- especially when they see it says 1995," said Diana Irving, 19, after picking up her diploma.
Some students signed petitions to protest the principal's removal. They argued over whether Mr. Durkin should leave, they witnessed a bitter fight among two groups of teachers -- one siding with Mr. Durkin and the other opposed -- and they saw administrators running out of the school building crying.
"This is our day to shine," said Rashema White, an 18-year-old graduate. "They messed up our whole day."
O'Neill Ellis, 17, learned Tuesday that he would not receive a diploma. He had failed an economics class, but Mr. Durkin allowed him to make up the credits by reading 15 chapters of an economic textbook and writing an 11-page report. When Mr. Ellis heard that Mr. Durkin had been removed, he suspected his diploma would be revoked, he said in an interview last night.
"I understand we might have messed up," he said. "But I don't see why they should have taken back our diplomas. It's not like I did a little two-page project. It took 11 pages, it required thinking."
He said that he knew he was behind in the class but that the teacher, whom he did not identify, refused to allow him to make up the work. He said he had received above-average grades in his other classes, with a 75 average, and said that he had got an 82 on the final economics exam.
Mr. Ellis said he had been accepted by Medgar Evers College in Brooklyn, where he will enroll to study business finance once he receives his diploma. He said he will attend summer school to make up the economics class.
Another student who failed the same economics class, Gilberto Gonzalez, received his diploma late yesterday afternoon. Mr. Durkin assigned him the same economics textbook assignment and gave him a passing grade for the class. Board officials agreed with the principal's assessment.
Mr. Gonzalez said the teacher had failed him because he was absent from school for more than a month, suffering from an ankle injury and asthma. In an interview outside the school, he said his teacher had refused to give him work to complete at home. He said he had received passing grades, with a 65 average, and said that he had gotten a 97 on the final economics exam.
After receiving his diploma, Mr. Gonzalez said, "I feel great, I feel so great. If it wasn't for Mr. Durkin giving me a second chance, I wouldn't have gotten my diploma."
An English teacher, Linda Winkler, described a case involving a student in her class. She said the principal gave a passing grade to a student who had been absent from her class at least 50 times since February.
She said the student, whom she did not identify, did little homework and refused to take the final exam. She said she gave the student a 45, out of 100, which is a failing grade. She later found out, she said, that the principal had assigned the student an independent project that involved writing a book report on the novel "To Sir With Love." The principal gave the student a 65 in the class. Ms. Winkler said this angered her because she had assigned four book reports to students during the term.
"I think it's wrong to reward a student in that position," she said. "In the past few years it has reached the point where the standards are so low that if things are barely legible, they are passable."
Several teachers have said they agree with her allegations that the interference was improper.
But Mr. Durkin's removal, pending a formal disciplinary hearing, spurred an outpouring of support for him yesterday.
Teachers, students and community leaders praised Mr. Durkin for improving the security and the academic standards at a school that once was considered one of the city's worst high schools. About 40 people gathered last night in the school's library to voice support for Mr. Durkin and organize opposition to his removal. Outside the school, some of Mr. Durkin's supporters posted signs saying, "Save our principal Mr. Durkin."
In a letter to Mr. Durkin, Gregg Lundahl, a social studies teacher, said, "I am shocked and outraged at the recent events. I believe that you have been character-assassinated by a handful of the faculty who have a bone to pick with you."
More than a dozen other teachers voiced their support for Mr. Durkin and said he was opposed by some faculty members because he tried to run a tighter ship.
"What I think went on here is that there are teachers who don't want to teach," said an English teacher, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "They fill the blackboard with vocabulary words and the kids are asleep, so he gives them an unsatisfactory rating. He makes them work."
Roz DeFelice, a health teacher who first made public the allegations against Mr. Durkin, denied that her motives were personal. "If I find a principal is breaking a rule, I have to report it," she said. "I reported what I saw. I don't know if I'm correct about it -- it's up to the board to investigate. They obviously found something."
Several students and teachers suggested Ms. DeFelice was conducting a personal vendetta against Mr. Durkin, whom many described as tough and, at times, dictatorial. One student said Ms. DeFelice routinely referred to Mr. Durkin as "Mr. Jerkin."
Ms. DeFelice said, "I do not like Mr. Durkin. Mr. Durkin knows it and everyone else knows it. But I did not make him break the law; he did that himself."
Ms. DeFelice said she believed Mr. Durkin had graduated the students because he wanted to improve the school's reputation so he would receive early tenure, which can be granted after three years of service. Some of the teachers suggested that Mr. Durkin, who became Washington Irving's principal in 1992, was to receive early tenure on June 30. But John J. Ferrandino, the superintendent in charge of the Division of High Schools and the person who would approve any granting of tenure, said Mr. Durkin was not among the candidates for early tenure.

Diplomas of the other 17 are being withheld. (Frances Roberts for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: What's Next: Ads on School Buses?;
In Tune With Giuliani, Agencies Ponder Billboard Revenue

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

 BODY:
Most people look at New York City's litter baskets, schoolhouses, parking meters, garbage trucks and the like and see the chattel of municipal government. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's administration sees a far more lucrative possibility: ad space.
And it's for sale.
The city's Parks and Recreation Department hopes to raise $10 million this year by selling ads on virtually anything in its domain other than rocks and trees. The Board of Education plans to offer the sides of yellow school buses for advertising. The Sanitation Department wants to sell prime space on 25,000 sidewalk trash cans -- and even its garbage trucks. 
 With a city strapped for cash, Mr. Giuliani has endorsed the proposals and included some in the new budget -- at least in part to offer some balm to soothe the sting of spending cuts. In fact, the Mayor's budget office has prodded agencies to think of almost any public space as a potential billboard.
And the ideas are pouring in: Ads on dog runs. Ads on municipal buildings. Ads on newsstands and on still more bus shelters to pay for public pay toilets that would also carry, yes, more ads. In these hard times, officials say anything is possible within still to be detailed bounds of reason and taste.
It would be an exaggeration to say the city's for sale, but "Your Ad Here" has never held so much potential on public property. At least a dozen ideas are now snaking through the approval process.
A few are far advanced, with others still in planning. The Board of Education plans to begin soliciting bids for the school buses any day now, with school buildings to follow. The Parks Department, the agency in the vanguard, is already reviewing offers to place ads on basketball backboards in playgrounds, and yesterday it collected general ideas from eight eager advertisers.
"The whole point of it is that during times of budgetary stress, one looks for creative ways to find sources of revenues that don't impact on the economy of the city," the Mayor's Budget Director, Marc V. Shaw, said. "Advertising is one of those areas." Some people, of course, are appalled. They say the plans represent a gross commercialization of public property, even in a city that is probably the most ad-soaked metropolis in America.
"New York City is a place where serenity is difficult to find as it is," said City Councilman Thomas K. Duane, a Manhattan Democrat, whose subcommittee plans to hold a hearing today on ads being considered for parks. "I don't think the city should pile on."
Mr. Duane and others vow to fight.
No sooner, for example, had the Parks Department proposed erecting advertising on the small triangle in Times Square occupied by the TKTS booth than a coalition formed to oppose it. Its leader, Jeffrey Katz, called the idea "an act of violence" to the statue of the Rev. Francis P. Duffy, the World War I chaplain, that lends its name to the triangle.
"I don't think you can think about putting an advertisement for underwear next to Father Duffy," said Mr. Katz, the president of Sherwood Equities, a company that owns buildings around Times Square.
The city and its related agencies have allowed advertising on municipal property for years -- from the subways and buses to the bus shelters, which now generate about $4 million a year. But with a Republican Mayor who champions "privatization," the concept has extended to places that remained untapped.
"There's a different aura now," said Lucian Chalfen, a spokesman for the Sanitation Department, which hopes to lure advertisers to its litter baskets and eventually its fleet of 2,100 garbage trucks.
Even the City Council has joined in. It is considering legislation to permit ads on independent public pay phones, just like Nynex. And Councilman Walter L. McCaffrey, a Queens Democrat, said the city should sell ads on parking meters, something that has been tried in Baltimore.
"Is there a point where you can have too much?" he said. "Yes, but we haven't reached that yet."
In the case of parks and schools in particular, the ads are intended to raise money that would otherwise have to be cut from services.
The Board of Education hopes to raise an estimated $5 million from ads on 2,000 of the yellow school buses -- something allowed for the first time as part of a new contract with private bus owners and drivers.
The board has cast a wide net to invite companies to place bids next month -- making it clear that only "soft advertising" would do. That means no guns, cigarettes, liquor or waifish, half-nude models. "Every dollar that we bring in," a board spokesman, John H. Beckman, said, "is a dollar that we can avoid cutting from a program to serve kids."
In another case, the city hopes the ads will pay for an amenity as scarce as advertising is abundant: public toilets.
As part of an effort to reduce clutter on sidewalks, the Mayor has proposed hiring one company to maintain all bus shelters and newsstands and build 30 new toilets in exchange for selling ads on the structures. The city's share of the take could total $8 million.
Deputy Mayor Fran Reiter, who is overseeing the proposal, said the city would in all cases strike a balance between revenues and exploitation, involving the public and elected officials in the debate over what is or is not appropriate.
"There are people -- I think a minority -- who are opposed to any street advertising," she said. "There are those who want more of it. I think there is this great middle ground."
It remains to be seen how lucrative the proposals could be, and for now the Mayor's office has no hard estimate of how much the ads could raise altogether. Kent L. Barwick, president of the Municipal Art Society of New York, which came of age opposing the proliferation of billboards 90 years ago, said the "limited amount" that might be made would not be worth it if the ads eroded the city's quality of life.
"This visual assault, if it's not carefully handled, will erode the qualities that make the city special," he said.

HEADLINE: Tax Defeat Makes Orange County's Fate More Perilous

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE 

 BODY:
Now that the voters of Orange County, Calif., have overwhelmingly said no to an emergency tax increase, it has become even trickier to resolve the severely pressed county's financial problems. And chances that the state of California might immediately step in to help appeared to be diminishing.
In a watershed vote on Tuesday, the electorate of the conservative Southern California area rejected, by a margin of more than 3 to 2, a proposed half-cent sales tax increase intended to lift the county out of bankruptcy. 
 The increase would have raised the combined state and county sales tax rate to 8.25 percent, from the current 7.75 percent. Without it, many in the municipal bond market contend, the financial situation in the county has gone from bad to worse, threatening to hurt the broader municipal bond market.
The tax was to have provided the county, which is short of cash, with $130 million in new revenue each year -- a key component in paying nearly $800 million in debts that come due in the next few months. Without the tax, it is unclear how the county will meet those debts, and yesterday, Standard & Poor's Credit Rating Service declared all that debt to be in default.
More important, perhaps, the vote was being viewed yesterday as a sign of a stubborn unwillingness on the part of this affluent area south of Los Angeles to honor its financial obligations after losing $1.7 billion last year in risky bond-market investments. The county filed for bankruptcy protection in December.
Paul Nussbaum, a Wells Fargo executive and assistant to the interim Orange County executive, William J. Popejoy, said that with the defeat of the sales-tax increase, debt holders might now receive less than full repayment.
"Obviously getting back 100 cents on the dollar is off the table," Mr. Nussbaum said. "This is turning more into a typical bankruptcy, where everyone develops a bunker mentality. After this vote, no one is going to remember Orange County just for Disneyland."
Many in the municipal bond market fear that Orange County's actions have begun to shake the market's foundations. Many California municipalities have already been forced to pay more to get investors to buy their debt, and there is concern that this pattern could spread in the $1.2 trillion municipal bond market. Munis have long been considered one of the safest forms of investments. Now, some worry that safety-oriented investors will shun the bonds, forcing states, counties, cities and special districts to offer higher interest rates to obtain essential financing.
In Sacramento, California's State Treasurer, Matthew Fong, said yesterday that it would be premature to begin considering state oversight of Orange County's finances, although demands for just that were increasing on Wall Street and among Orange County's debt holders.
One idea being discussed was the establishment of a fiscal control board similar to the Municipal Assistance Corporation of New York, which would issue new bonds to pay the county's debts and back them with new taxes.
A proposal from a California State Senator, Lucy Killea of San Diego, that had been passed by the Legislature would have replaced the Orange County Board of Supervisors with a powerful county executive and diverted some property-tax revenue to repay new debts. But Gov. Pete Wilson vetoed the bill, and though it is being prepared for reconsideration, hopes are fading that the Governor and the State Treasurer will embrace the new proposal.
"The critical mass for a state takeover is not there," Mr. Fong said. "Orange County has given the state a black eye, but it's not a knockout blow. Because of that, it is premature to talk of a state takeover."
Mr. Fong added that if current debt holders agreed to a proposed rollover plan and extended the maturity of their short-term debt by a year, the county would gain some breathing room to explore new options. One hope, he said, would be to collect from the county's broker, Merrill Lynch & Company, which it has sued over the investment losses. Or, he said, "whatever else the county supervisors can conjure up." Asked for possible details, Mr. Fong replied, "That's a good question and there's no obvious answer."
Merrill Lynch said yesterday that Orange County should not count on proceeds from any lawsuit as part of its recovery plan. "We are very confident of our legal position, and we do not think there is a basis for Merrill Lynch to pay for problems it did not create," Timothy Gillis, a spokesman for the brokerage firm, said.
Many municipal experts said that even if Orange County did collect money from Merrill, such protracted litigation could not provide the quick fix the county needed.
Without the sales tax, municipal-finance experts see Orange County's situation as dire. "They are fiddling while Rome is burning," remarked Joseph Mysak, editor of Grant's Municipal Bond Observer. "This story goes from worse to worse to worse. California is displaying real political indecision from top to bottom, and it shows just how fragile the California markets are. Orange County is trading one of the real cornerstones of the municipal market -- the full faith and credit promise of general-obligation debt."
Jane Eddy, an analyst with Standard & Poor's, said the voters' rejection of the tax increase had created a "real disaster." She added, "The county has no other financial options, and even if the debt holders agree to roll over their debt, there is nothing to indicate that they will be paid a year from now." For that reason, Standard & Poor's declared the debt in default.
Moody's Investors Service, another rating service whose judgment is key to the sale of municipal bonds, called the county's action "outrageous and unprecedented." In a written statement, Moody's said, "Orange County has utterly failed to take responsibility for its own actions and their consequences."
Karen Krop, an assistant vice president at Moody's, said that given the well-known antitax views of Orange County residents, even a state takeover might not provide the answer. If some of the county's property taxes were diverted to pay its debts, she said, "you can imagine the kind of uproar that you would hear."
In the next two months, the county is obligated to repay $800 million in short-term notes, and holders of these notes expressed interest yesterday in state intervention in the hope that they would be paid in full.
Holders of $600 million in taxable notes, which the county has threatened to repudiate by challenging their legality, met in New York yesterday. A spokesman for that group termed the situation "very grave" and said the noteholders were disappointed by the sales tax rejection.
Bennett J. Murphy, a Los Angeles lawyer who represents Orange County bondholders, said the vote "puts it back at square one."
If the county fails to repay the creditors fully, he added, "it has to be prepared for a long serious drought" before ever borrowing again.
Robert Moore, a Chicago lawyer who heads the overall creditors' committee, called for a state oversight board. "If they are going to act, now is the time," Mr. Moore said. "We are facing great uncertainty."

HEADLINE: Agreement Is Reached On Allowing Casinos

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: ALBANY, June 28

 BODY:
State legislative leaders said today that they had reached agreement on a constitutional amendment that would allow casino gambling in designated parts of the state, including the Catskills and Niagara Falls but excluding New York City.
The leaders said they expect the resolution, the first step in a long process of removing the State Constitution's prohibition on casino gambling, to pass in both houses when it comes to a vote on Thursday. 
 "I predict it will pass because it's the common sense thing to do," said Joseph L. Bruno, a Republican, the Senate majority leader. Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, a Manhattan Democrat, said, "I am fairly confident it will pass in my House."
But though support from the legislative leaders usually guarantees passage of a bill, some proponents of the amendment expressed concerns about its fate in the Republican-controlled Senate, where Democratic votes will be needed for passage.
"We'll have a major floor fight and it's going to be a close vote," said Senator Nicholas A. Spano, a Westchester Republican, a leading advocate of the resolution.
Late today, Senate Democratic officials said Mr. Bruno had not shown them the language in the bill, had not included them in negotiations and had made no effort to win their votes on the resolution, even though the Legislature is scheduled to adjourn for the summer by Friday.
The agreement reached between Mr. Bruno, a Rensselaer County Republican, and Mr. Silver would allow casinos in four places: the Catskills, the City of Niagara Falls, the City of Buffalo, and Warren and Saratoga Counties near Lake George.
The agreement calls for one casino in each place except for the three Catksill counties, Greene, Ulster and Sullivan, where a larger, as yet undetermined number of casinos would be authorized.
The resolution would also allow eight harness and thoroughbred horse-racing tracks around the state to have slot machines and video gambling. But Belmont and Aqueduct race tracks would be excluded.
The agreement does not allow casinos in New York City, despite some 11th-hour lobbying efforts by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani to have the city included. Mr. Giuliani called the omission baffling today, adding that the Legislature was "making certain that New York City doesn't get its fair share of revenues."
Mr. Silver said he had problems with allowing casinos in New York City because the city would become "the casino capital of the state" and hurt the viability of casinos in areas like the Catskills. But he also said the Republican Mayor, with whom he has clashed repeatedly, did not call him for support and never offered a specific proposal.
To amend the State Constitution, two consecutively elected Legislatures must pass a resolution calling for a statewide referendum. That vote could not take place until 1997 at the earliest. Under the proposed amendment, a casino could not open until the county involved also held a referendum.
Federal law allows casinos on Indian reservations. In New York State, the Oneida Indians operate a casino near Syracuse, and the Mohawk Indians plan to open more near the Canadian border.
With the resolution's fate still in doubt, lobbyists on both sides of the issue buttonholed Legislators today, offering a tiny hint of the hugely expensive battle certain to ensue if the resolution passes.
On one side, lobbyists representing hotels, tourism businesses and race tracks warned lawmakers that tourism dollars are being siphoned away from New York State by surrounding states with casinos.
On the other side, religious groups including the New York State Catholic Conference, which represents the state's Roman Catholic bishops, circulated memos arguing that casinos will increase crime, drug use, prostitution and political corruption.
State Comptroller H. Carl McCall warned that the economic growth promised by gambling proponents could be illusory, and he called for further study.
"If you see first passage now, there will be very little discussion about it until the same time next year," said Mr. McCall, a Democrat.
But Mr. Silver argued that first passage was needed now to encourage casino companies to consider New York as a place to invest. "We should do it so people don't commit their resources elsewhere," he said.
Gov. George E. Pataki also said today there was a strong likelihood the Legislature would approve the first increase in minimum liability rates for drivers since 1957. The Governor favors increasing the minimum coverage to $25,000 for bodily injury to one person, $50,000 for total injuries to more than one person and $10,000 for property damage. Current numbers $10,000, $20,000 and $5,000.
The insurance industry has opposed the bill, saying it would lead to higher auto insurance rates and increase the number of uninsured drivers. Trial lawyers, along with many consumer and auto safety groups, support the bill, saying it raises absurdly out-dated payments.
In other action, the Assembly, by 141-8, passed a bill tonight that would require convicted sex offenders to register with the state when released from prison.
The Senate has already passed the bill, and Mr. Pataki has promised to sign it into law.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Will Presidents miss a few economic aides? Some say yes.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
WHEN the President wants economic advice, he can always turn to his National Economic Council, the Office of Management and Budget or the Cabinet agencies. So why not jettison the Council of Economic Advisers, a 48-year-old symbol of an era when economists had the hubris to believe they could smooth the way to permanent prosperity?
Such reasoning, spiced with more than a dash of partisanship, apparently explains the House Republicans' inclination to deny funds for the council. But in the view of conservative as well as liberal economists, it represents myopia with a capital M. "It would be a pity to throw away a bargain," argues Martin S. Feldstein, chairman of the council under President Ronald Reagan. 
 As Federal agencies go, the council, with a professional staff of 27 and a payroll of less than $4 million, barely rates an asterisk in the trillion-dollar-plus Federal budget. Still, it seemed a plausible enough target for Republicans determined to match cuts in Congressional staff with trims in executive branch operations.
The Clinton Administration attacked the Republican effort again yesterday as it named a new chairman to the council, Joseph E. Stiglitz, who replaces Laura D'Andrea Tyson. Still, Republicans on a House Appropriations subcommittee voted yesterday to eliminate the council.
If the Republicans succeed, the Republic will no doubt survive. For one thing, the once-primary role of the council in balancing the risks of recession and inflation has fallen to the keepers of the money supply at the Federal Reserve: The ability to use tax and spending policy to fine-tune economic growth has withered in an era of $200 billion-plus budget deficits. And in any event, the President would still be able to staff the White House with professional economists.
But Mr. Feldstein, now the head of the National Bureau of Economic Research, thinks much would be lost in downgrading the job. "The council has built a remarkable reputation," he said. "There's just no way you could recruit comparably qualified economists" without the nameplate on the office door.
Michael J. Boskin, the council chairman under President George Bush, acknowledges that the Cabinet agencies employ some fine economists. But council economists, largely recruited from academia, "bring a fresh look," he argues, "one free of institutional bias." You need outsiders, he suggests, to settle differences between, say, the Environmental Protection Agency and the Energy Department.
Then, too, even the most ambitious members of the council tend to be economists first and politicians second. For while partisanship sometimes colors their public pronouncements, most council members understand they will not be in government for long. "They are under pressure from the profession to retain their intellectual independence," says James Tobin, a member of the council in the Kennedy White House.
The benefits of this intellectual independence are not hard to find. In 1961 Mr. Tobin convinced a skeptical President John F. Kennedy of the virtues of tax incentives for business investment. In 1967 Arthur M. Okun tried to persuade President Lyndon B. Johnson to pay for the Vietnam War with a tax increase -- a policy shift that would likely have contained the inflationary fires to come. And in 1982, Mr. Feldstein fought the good fight against slashing taxes before taking on government spending.
By the same token, council economists, liberal and conservative, have manned the battlements against ill-justified interference with free markets. Council economists under President Reagan held back the tide of trade protectionism. In 1990 the council fought for market-based incentives to cut the cost of containing acid rain. In 1993, Ms. Tyson warned the White House of the striking similarities between the organization of the Clinton health care bureaucracy and the Bulgarian ministry of farm machinery and shoe polish.
A less appreciated benefit of the council has been to raise the quality of policy economics. A discipline that accords its highest honors to theorists or ideologues and disdains hands-on experience would have much less of value to say, for example, about ways to contain inflation or the pros and cons of deregulating the electric power industry. "Just compare American economists to their European counterparts," suggests Richard L. Schmalensee, a former council member who now teaches at M.I.T.
The council has been on the endangered species list before. After Mr. Feldstein went public with his critique of voodoo economics, the Reagan White House vowed to rid itself of apostates. But that wound was self-inflicted. Congress is now threatening to deprive future Presidents of easy access to top-rank economic analysis. And, in the words of Mr. Schmalensee, merely "to save the cost of the radio on one stealth bomber."

HEADLINE: Cravath Lawyer and Brother Are Guilty of Insider Trading

BYLINE: By PETER TRUELL 

 BODY:
A former senior associate at Cravath, Swaine & Moore, the blue-chip Wall Street law firm, and his brother, pleaded guilty yesterday to insider trading in a case that shows how even well-paid young lawyers can fall prey to the temptation to pass on confidential information.
With the recent upswing in big mergers and takeovers, there has been a steady increase in insider trading cases, some of them involving lawyers and investment bankers and their family members. But yesterday's guilty plea is the first such case to involve Cravath, a firm that represents many of the biggest American companies, and that has prided itself on its clean record. 
 The former Cravath senior associate, Richard W. Woodward, 34, was accused of passing along confidential information on a dozen prospective mergers and acquisitions to his brother, John T. Woodward, 36. The brothers each pleaded guilty to one count of insider trading in Federal District Court in Manhattan. The two also settled a complaint brought by the Securities and Exchange Commission and agreed to pay back $110,000 of their profits.
According to Federal investigators, Richard Woodward, a resident of Middletown, N.J., joined Cravath in 1989 and worked as a skilled and valued lawyer on much of its most important merger and acquisition business. For several years, even as the law firm regarded him as a hard-working and successful employee, Mr. Woodward passed information on deals involving such important Cravath clients as CBS, ITT, Johnson & Johnson, King World Productions and S. G. Warburg from 1990 to 1995.
John Woodward, a 36-year-old electrical supply salesman from Little Silver, N.J., who is currently unemployed, used this information to trade in the securities markets, the United States Attorney's Office said. He made profits of about $578,000 by buying the securities of 12 companies that were either Cravath clients or possible merger partners for these clients. These purchases were made in advance of possible merger and acquisition announcements. Another unnamed friend of Richard Woodward's and other unnamed people that he "caused to trade" made profits of about $355,000.
The United States Attorney's Office in Manhattan and the S.E.C. declined to identify these people but said their investigations were continuing.
The indictment detailed a dozen cases involving insider trading by the Woodward brothers. A typical example was the proposed acquisition of Vista Chemical by the AMC subsidiary of a German Company, RWE-DEA A.G. In February 1990, AMC retained Cravath in connection with a tender offer it was considering for Vista Chemical. Richard Woodward was among the team of Cravath lawyers assigned to represent AMC and therefore obtained "confidential, material and nonpublic information concerning the transaction," the United States Attorney's Office said.
A few months later, Richard Woodward "disclosed the potential tender offer" to his brother, John, who around Nov. 30 bought about 2,000 Vista Chemical shares for about $25 a share. AMC announced its tender offer on Dec. 13, causing Vista Chemical's stock price to rise to $54 a share from $29 a share. John Woodward then sold his Vista Chemical stock for a profit of about $57,000.
The insider trading became apparent to regulators earlier this year, according to the S.E.C. and Cravath. The National Association of Securities Dealers was investigating unusual trading in the shares of a company that had been acquired in 1994 by a Cravath client. It first notified Cravath of its inquiry in January and later passed on a list of stockholders who had traded the stock before the announcement of the deal. The law firm said it noticed the name John T. Woodward and confronted Richard Woodward.
"We fired him the same day we called the S.E.C.," said Samuel C. Butler, presiding partner at Cravath. "It's a tragedy, on the one hand I feel terribly sorry for him, his wife and his children, but on the other hand, I feel outraged at this breach of trust."
In a statement, Cravath said that all clients mentioned in the indictment and S.E.C. complaint were notified as soon as the firm learned that transactions in which they were involved might be under investigation. The two brothers, who have cooperated with the Government, are scheduled to be sentenced on Sept. 15 on the felony charge, which carries a maximum sentence of five years in prison.
The insider trading case illustrates how one employee can for several years, without detection, operate a systematic scheme to profit from inside information involving a dozen major companies. The current takeover boom means that there will almost certainly be more such cases as some professionals find themselves unable to resist the chance of quick and easy, but illegal, profits, either for themselves or for their friends and families.
"The general feeling is that we're seeing more insider trading investigations now than we've seen since the takeover boom of the 1980's," said Daniel A. Nathan, an assistant director in the S.E.C.'s enforcement division. "In terms of filed cases we're ahead of where we were a year ago," he added, saying that in the 12 months ended October 1994, the S.E.C. had filed about 45 such cases, compared with 30-plus in the year earlier.
At yesterday's hearing before Judge Lawrence M. McKenna, Richard Woodward "acknowledged that he had disclosed matters on which he'd worked to his brother," his lawyer, Mark R. Hellerer, said.
Richard Woodward recognized it was reckless of him to do so, and that it breached his duty to Cravath and to his clients. He said Cravath was not involved in any way and sincerely apologized."

HEADLINE: Space Linkup With $100 Billion Future

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD 

DATELINE: HOUSTON, June 28

 BODY:
If the linkup of Americans and Russians in space two decades ago was the equivalent of a date, the one set for Thursday is the start of a $100 billion marriage, a delicate one that could easily be undone by technical setbacks or political shifts.
The space shuttle Atlantis is to dock with the Russian space station Mir about 9 A.M., hooking up a pressurized tunnel through which astronauts can float in shirt-sleeves. If successfully joined, the two craft will form a structure bigger and brighter than any other to probe the heavens in the 38 years since the space age began. 
 The joined spacecraft will have a record mass of about 200 tons and will hold a record 10 people -- 6 Americans and 4 Russians, 8 men and 2 women. The five-day linkup features biomedical research and a crew swap. More important, it foreshadows bigger things to come.
Over the next two years, the American winged spaceships and Mir are to dock seven times in preparation for building a 460-ton international space station larger than a football field. The orbital base is to house Americans, Russians, Japanese, Europeans and Canadians who will do science experiments, observe the heavens and the Earth and study their responses to weightlessness in the hopes of learning how humans might one day survive trips to Mars and beyond.
The linkup will not reveal the ultimate fate of the East-West marriage and its offspring. But experts say it will test the relationship technically because of the docking's complexity and politically because of growing tensions between Washington and Moscow.
"We're embarking on a very challenging technical and political feat," Daniel S. Goldin, head of the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, said in an interview. "To deny both issues is to deny reality."
Before liftoff on Tuesday, Captain Robert L. Gibson of the Navy, the shuttle commander, said the docking was "the biggest challenge we're facing," adding that the mission was "very ambitious." That is astronaut talk for perilous.
In space, the laws of mass and momentum, unfettered by such earthly influences as weight and friction, are unforgiving and ready to punish a false step by sending spacecraft crashing into one another other, endangering astronauts. Whether in orbit or deep space, objects set in motion tend to stay that way, never slowing down except when encountering an opposing force or another object.
With such perils in mind, American and Russian officials have framed the docking as an exceptionally slow ballet, with the final closing rate between the partners set at about one inch per second. Astronauts in simulators have practiced missteps and recoveries endlessly to give them as much experience as possible in sidestepping disaster.
"We've tried to predict everything," Viktor Legostaev, a top Russian designer for the mission, said in an interview. "But who knows? Maybe we will have problems."
The Russians are nervous because in 1975 the second of two dockings between the relatively puny Apollo and Soyuz capsules was accompanied by a jarring bump, undisclosed at the time. At Apollo's controls was Donald K. Slayton, flying slightly fast and off center.
"The force was twice what we predicted," Mr. Legostaev said. "There was no damage. But we thought about things breaking, because of the torques and forces."
The Apollo capsule carried three Americans and had a mass of about 20 tons, while the Soyuz carried two Russians and had a mass of about 7 tons -- paltry sums by today's standards. The Atlantis carries seven astronauts and has a mass of about 100 tons while the Mir has three astronauts and has about the same mass.
After docking for two days, the Apollo and Soyuz separated with their respective crew members. The docking had been arranged by the Nixon Administration during a temporary thaw in the cold war. It was a one-time spectacular, after which good feelings quickly dissipated.
In contrast, the docking of Atlantis and Mir is to be the start of a virtual merger of the manned space programs of East and West, involving thousands of ground personnel, hundreds of projects, dozens of astronauts and a flurry of dockings.
After the current mission, the American winged spaceships and Mir are to link up once more this year, three times in 1996 and twice in 1997, mainly as warmups for the international space station.
Assembly flights for the big space outpost are to begin in late 1997 and end in 2002, after which it is to operate for a decade or more. As envisioned, the station will have seven laboratories, house six astronauts and be 361 feet wide and 290 feet long.
A foreign-policy pillar of the Clinton Administration, the international station is meant to symbolize a new era of East-West accord and to engage Moscow in constructive space work in return for ending practices that upset Washington, like exporting advanced rocket gear to developing countries.
The deal includes financial aid to Moscow in the form of a $400 million contract for space hardware and launching services, which is seen by Washington as a way to help prop up Moscow's space program as the nation moves toward a more open society and economy.
NASA says the space station will cost American taxpayers $43 billion, with contributions from foreign users pushing the total cost as high as $60 billion. But some Congressional estimates put the figure at $100 billion. Those figures do not include the more than 70 launchings of gear and people needed for its assembly, outfitting and utilization.
Congressional investigators at the General Accounting Office recently estimated that the American part of the international station, with the inclusion of $50.5 billion in shuttle transportation costs, would total $93.9 billion. NASA disagreed with that total, saying the shuttle would fly even if the international station were canceled.
Also not included in the total is the expense of the shuttle-Mir linkups, which NASA says are relatively minor. For instance, the docking system of Atlantis cost $95 million. NASA says shuttle flights cost $400 million apiece. Independent experts say they often cost twice that amount.
The linkups and the space station currently enjoy bipartisan support on Capitol Hill. But private analysts say the venture might suffer amid growing Federal budget battles and the ambivalence among members of Congress about aiding Russia.
"It's not too hard to see how the mood could change with unexpected suddenness," said John E. Pike, head of space policy at the Federation of American Scientists, a private group in Washington that backs the East-West endeavor.
Some scientists oppose the station, saying it threatens to gobble up a growing share of the shrinking Federal science budget, eroding the nation's ability to meet future challenges in fields ranging from communications to medical research.
But other experts say the space station and the Shuttle-Mir linkups are important not only for space exploration but as an investment in global peace, helping Russia become a responsible member of the global community.
"We're always going to have differences with them, just like we do with Japan," said Thomas P. Stafford, the American astronaut who led the Apollo-Soyuz linkup 20 years ago, and whose own docking, the first, went smoothly.
"But in the long run," he added, "for stability in the world, we're going to work with Russia and we're going to have good relations with them."


GRAPHIC: The docking of the American space shuttle Atlantis with the Russian Mir Space station is the first of seven such missions that are to pave the way for assembling a multinational space station. It is a larger and much more complicated job than the Soyuz-Apollo linup achieved two decades ago (shown to scale below).
Diagram: "A CLOSER LOOK: A Complex Linkup In Space"
 The Mir and the shuttle each weigh about 100 tons. The new project involves maneuvering the shuttle very showly, at some points about an inch a second, to line up its docking mechanism with that the the Mir. Then, two inches away, the shuttle fires its jets to complete the linkup. Each docking assembly has three "petals" that interleave like a flower to form the link. Twelve sets of hooks and latches on the shuttle mechanism grip a similar assembly on the Mir. The entire docking and latching process is expected to take about an hour, beginning at 9 A.M., Eastern standard time. (Sources: NASA; Rockwell International)

HEADLINE: A DEAL ON AUTO TRADE: THE JAPANESE;
Number of Parts to Be Bought Is Unclear

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Thursday, June 29

 BODY:
While President Clinton praised the auto trade agreement for pushing Japan's automobile industry to buy more American-made parts, there are no concrete goals for how much in American or foreign parts the Japanese companies will actually buy.
Securing such goals had been a main Washington objective, and there seems to be a big discrepancy between what American officials said the new trade agreement will do and what the Japanese company plans actually say. 
 President Clinton said Wednesday that the agreement will lead to an increase in purchases of American parts of almost $9 billion, or 50 percent, in three years.
But that is a United States Government estimate of what the agreement will accomplish. The Japanese Government said it had no involvement with such figures and did not agree to any targets.
"We didn't cave in," said a jubilant Japanese trade official.
Instead of setting goals for parts purchases, the Japanese companies promised to take such steps as working more closely with potential foreign suppliers or publishing guidebooks describing their procurement procedures.
The Honda Motor Company, for instance, promised to "further increase local procurement though a focused effort to purchase more parts for American-made engines and transmissions," including shafts, gears, sensors and other parts it specifies. The Nissan Motor Company said it would set up an in-house "imports promotion committee" to increase imports of parts.
Such procedural steps agreed to in past trade agreements have not always led to increased sales of American products in Japan. That is the main reason the Clinton Administration had insisted the automobile trade agreement would have to be "results oriented."
Setting such goals also seems to work. The Japanese companies have largely achieved a goal set in 1992 to double purchases of American parts to $19 billion by the fiscal year that ended in March.
Much of the effort announced in the plans seems to be aimed at increasing procurement of parts for use in the companies' overseas factories. There is not as much language devoted to increasing imports of foreign parts into Japan. In that sense, the agreement might not go far toward achieving one of Washington's major goals -- breaking up the keiretsu system, in which the auto manufacturers buy mainly from their long-term Japanese suppliers.
Still, the fact that the Japanese companies did not announce specific targets does not necessarily mean they will not significantly increase their purchases of American parts. Because of the yen's rise, it makes economic sense for them to purchase more foreign parts. And the new trade agreement might be the excuse they need to switch from their Japanese keiretsu suppliers.
Regarding the issue of increasing the number of Japanese car dealers that sell American vehicles, the manufacturers generally said they would reiterate to their dealers that they were free to carry other brands and would set up mechanisms for handling grievances from manufacturers that feel they are being denied access to dealers.
Regarding sales of replacement parts, Honda said it would set up a new division to procure such parts from abroad and sell them through its dealers. Toyota said it would study setting up a new company to do the same thing.
All the plans announced last night talk about expansion of production and procurement in Europe and Asia as well as North America. This is in part because European and Asian nations have expressed alarm that an agreement between the United States and Japan would favor American suppliers at the expense of European or Asian companies.
The one area in which the Japanese companies often mentioned specific numbers was for increased production of cars or engines in the United States. The United States calculated the five companies would increase their automobile output in the United States from 2.1 million to 2.65 million cars by 1998. The companies also planned to increase their exports of American-made cars to Japan and elsewhere.
But many of the plans for increased American production had already been announced or were being contemplated because the rise of the Japanese yen has forced the Japanese companies to shift manufacturing away from their own country.
The plan announced by Honda, for instance, contains nothing new, a spokesman, Yo Harada, said, explaining that the document mainly repeats commitments to increase North American production, announced last July. The Toyota Motor Corporation, however, promised a new increase in North American production.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Last-Minute Deal With Japan Increases the Dollar's Value

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
The 11th-hour agreement between the United States and Japan on trade in autos and auto parts was greeted with enthusiasm on world currency markets yesterday, as dealers bought dollars and sold yen, relieved that a damaging -- and possibly widespread -- trade war had been averted.
"A cloud has been lifted," said Richard E. Witten, a partner at Goldman, Sachs & Company. "Nobody likes trade wars." 
 The possibility of a full-scale trade conflict has been one reason for the dollar's weakness against the yen this year, so when word that a settlement had been reached flashed across traders' computer screens yesterday, it was not surprising to see the dollar increase in value.
Late in New York, the dollar rose to 85.52 Japanese yen from 84.29 yen on Tuesday. It also rose to 1.3968 German marks from 1.3870.
It will be some time before the agreement leads to a narrowing in the American trade deficit with Japan, and indeed the terms and effect of the deal seemed in question late yesterday. Still, analysts said that an end to the sniping that has gone on between Washington and Tokyo over auto trade was likely to allow currency traders to focus on other issues -- ones that favored a rise in the dollar's value.
"To the degree that trade frictions between the two countries have been relieved, the political motivation for an ever-rising yen and an ever-falling dollar has been relieved," said Neal M. Soss of Soss & Cotton Enterprises, a New York economic consultant. "And if you add the notion that the dollar is grossly undervalued against both the yen and the mark, then you could see further dollar appreciation."
Scott E. Pardee, vice chairman of Yamaichi Securities International, said that even though trade disputes involving Eastman Kodak and Federal Express remained unresolved, the nature of those quarrels with the Japanese, and the manner in which they were ultimately resolved, was not likely to concern currency markets. Compared with autos, the amounts of money involved were much smaller, he said.
More than half the $66 billion deficit the United States ran with Japan last year was in autos and related products.
Now, currency traders "will be looking more closely at economic fundamentals," he said. At the moment, those fundamentals include an extremely wide difference between short-term interest rates in Japan and those in the United States, as well as continued weakness in the Japanese economy.
"The only reason to hold yen with interest rates in Japan at 1 percent instead of investing the money in 6 percent short-term U.S. rates is you think the yen is going to appreciate," Mr. Soss said. "That could turn into an argument for a weaker currency."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Prices Rise After Trade Accord Spurs Dollar

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities bounced higher yesterday, erasing early losses after a breakthrough in trade negotiations with the Japanese buoyed the dollar.
Before that, prices were down as the market geared up for a five-year note auction after a mediocre two-year auction on Tuesday. 
 The market has had many concerns these days, among them the United States-Japanese trade negotiations, which went to the wire. Other concerns have been the supply of new debt issues coming to the market and whether the Federal Reserve will lower interest rates at the meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee less than a week away.
The 30-year bond rose 17/32 , to a price of 11418/32 for a yield of 6.52 percent, down from 6.54 percent on Tuesday.
James Hale, a fixed-income analyst with MMS International in San Francisco, said the successful trade negotiation and subsequent surge in the dollar were key factors in the market's about-face. "But even after rebounding on trade-agreement news, trading was still somewhat cautious going into the five-year note," he said.
The auction of five-year notes turned out to be a well-sold affair, even with retail interest from small investors somewhat subdued. Traders said the $11.5 billion of five-year notes were priced for a high yield of 5.90 percent, the lowest in almost a year and a half, when it was 5.61 percent on Feb. 24, 1994. Last month the yield was 6.25 percent.
Analysts say yields are moving lower amid expectations that the Fed will lower rates to help bolster the economy.
Sung Won Sohn, chief economist for the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis, believes the Fed chairman, Alan Greenspan, will move to cut interest rates next week.
"Chairman Greenspan desperately wants to achieve a soft landing and prevent the economy from skidding into a recession," Mr. Sohn said. "The Federal funds rate will be cut by half a point. The prime rate and other loan rates should follow."
Then there is the political factor, Mr. Sohn said. "In a democratic society, Mr. Greenspan cannot ignore political reality," Mr. Sohn said. "The White House wants to lower interest rates; President Clinton doesn't want to go back home to Arkansas in 1997."
Mr. Sohn said Congress was likely to cut about $50 billion from 1996 spending. "There has always been an unwritten quid pro quo between Congress and the Federal Reserve Board," he said. "Interest rates will be cut if Congress trims spending."
Even with the market holding its gains and feeling good mainly because of the dollar, Mr. Hale said trading was likely to be cautious heading into a holiday weekend and ahead of the Fed meeting next week, as well as the employment report next Friday.
"I am 99 percent sure the Fed will go with the rate cut at the meeting and not wait for the employment number," Mr. Hale said. There is no consensus on how much the cut will be, he said, but it could be 25 or 50 basis points. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Mr. Sohn said there was a risk for stocks and bonds. "If interest rates are not cut either in July or August, both markets will be disappointed," he said. "Interest rates would rise, hurting bonds. Projections for corporate earnings would be scaled back, damaging stocks."
Analysts said import-export prices for May, released yesterday, put pressure in early trading, as import prices rose 1 percent and exports three-tenths of a percent, both causing inflation flags to flutter.
Municipal bonds were steady yesterday with some investors reluctant to buy tax-exempt securities after voters in Orange County, Calif., voted down a tax increase to prevent default on its debt.
Traders said investors were concerned that other municipalities might decide to walk away from their debt through bankruptcy filings. The average yield to maturity for the Bond Buyer's index of 40 municipal issues rose a basis point, to 6.17 percent.
Some California borrowers paid the price for that concern yesterday. Alameda County, Calif., sold $85 million in one-year notes at prices to yield 6.40 percent, 30 basis points more than same-maturity Philadelphia notes and 25 basis points more than Colorado paper, both rated lower.
In the corporate debt market, corporations have sold 61 percent more bonds in the second quarter, as the sharp drop in interest rates cheapened the cost of borrowing in the financial markets.
The Standard & Poor's Ratings Group said companies issued $68.9 billion in bonds in the three-month period, up from the first quarter's $42.8 billion.
Industrial companies led the surge in new bonds, nearly doubling their sales in the period to $20.4 billion.
